<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Apostles%27_Creed" title="wikisource:Apostles' Creed">Apostles' Creed</a></b></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="38"/></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="wikisource:Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a></b></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Concise doctrinal statements or confessions of religious beliefs are known as creeds (from Latin <i>credo</i>, meaning "I believe"). They began as baptismal formulae and were later expanded during the <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christological</a> controversies of the 4th and 5th centuries to become statements of faith.</p>
<p>Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical" title="Evangelical">evangelical</a> Protestants reject creeds as definitive statements of faith, even while agreeing with some or all of the substance of the creeds. <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">The Baptists</a> have been non-creedal "in that they have not sought to establish binding authoritative confessions of faith on one another."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avis_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Avis-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:p.111</sup> Also rejecting creeds are groups with roots in the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_Movement" title="Restoration Movement">Restoration Movement</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church_(Disciples_of_Christ)" title="Christian Church (Disciples of Christ)">Christian Church (Disciples of Christ)</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Christian_Church_in_Canada" title="Evangelical Christian Church in Canada">Evangelical Christian Church in Canada</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Churches_of_Christ" title="Churches of Christ">Churches of Christ</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:14–15</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhodes_2005_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rhodes_2005-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:123</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nicaea_icon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1388" data-file-width="1024" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nicaea_icon.jpg/220px-Nicaea_icon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nicaea_icon.jpg/330px-Nicaea_icon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nicaea_icon.jpg/440px-Nicaea_icon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nicaea_icon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christian</a> icon depicting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I_and_Christianity" title="Constantine I and Christianity">Emperor Constantine</a> and the Fathers of the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> (325) as holding the Niceno–Constantinopolitan Creed of 381</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Creed" title="Apostles' Creed">Apostles' Creed</a> is the most widely accepted statement of the articles of Christian faith. It is used by a number of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christian_denominations" title="List of Christian denominations">Christian denominations</a> for both <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catechesis" title="Catechesis">catechetical</a> purposes, most visibly by liturgical churches of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Christian" title="Western Christian">Western Christian</a> tradition, including the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Rite_Orthodoxy" title="Western Rite Orthodoxy">Western Rite Orthodoxy</a>. It is also used by <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregationalists</a>. This particular creed was developed between the 2nd and 9th centuries. Its central doctrines are those of the Trinity and God the Creator. Each of the doctrines found in this creed can be traced to statements current in the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Age" title="Apostolic Age">apostolic period</a>. The creed was apparently used as a summary of Christian doctrine for baptismal candidates in the churches of Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Its main points include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Belief in <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">God the Father</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God" title="Son of God">Son of God</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">Holy Spirit</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">death</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harrowing_of_Hell" title="Harrowing of Hell">descent into hell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus" title="Ascension of Jesus">ascension</a> of Christ</li>
<li>The holiness of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecclesia_(church)" title="Ecclesia (church)">Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Communion_of_saints" title="Communion of saints">communion of saints</a></li>
<li>Christ's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_coming" title="Second coming">second coming</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Day of Judgement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salvation_in_Christianity" title="Salvation in Christianity">salvation</a> of the faithful.</li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a> was formulated, largely in response to <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arianism</a>, at the Councils of <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">Nicaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">Constantinople</a> in 325 and 381 respectively <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup> and ratified as the universal creed of <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Ephesus" title="First Council of Ephesus">First Council of Ephesus</a> in 431.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ephesus_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-ephesus-38">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Chalcedonian_Definition" title="Chalcedonian Definition">Chalcedonian Definition</a>, or Creed of Chalcedon, developed at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> in 451,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> though rejected by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodox" title="Oriental Orthodox">Oriental Orthodox</a> churches,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup> taught Christ "to be acknowledged in two natures, inconfusedly, unchangeably, indivisibly, inseparably": one divine and one human, and that both natures, while perfect in themselves, are nevertheless also <a href="/wiki/Hypostatic_union" title="Hypostatic union">perfectly united into one person</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Athanasian_Creed" title="Athanasian Creed">Athanasian Creed</a>, received in the Western Church as having the same status as the Nicene and Chalcedonian, says: "We worship one God in Trinity, and Trinity in Unity; neither confounding the Persons nor dividing the <a href="/wiki/Consubstantiality" title="Consubstantiality">Substance</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most Christians (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodox" title="Oriental Orthodox">Oriental Orthodox</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> alike) accept the use of creeds, and subscribe to at least one of the creeds mentioned above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UMC.E2.80.94Our_Common_Heritage_as_Christians_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-UMC.E2.80.94Our_Common_Heritage_as_Christians-43">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jesus">Jesus</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CompositeJesus.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="398" data-file-width="272" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/CompositeJesus.JPG/200px-CompositeJesus.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/CompositeJesus.JPG 1.5x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CompositeJesus.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Various <a href="/wiki/Depiction_of_Jesus" title="Depiction of Jesus">depictions of Jesus</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus in Christianity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christ_(title)" title="Christ (title)">Christ (title)</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_comparative_mythology" title="Jesus in comparative mythology">Jesus in comparative mythology</a></div>
<p>The central tenet of Christianity is the belief in <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God_(Christianity)" title="Son of God (Christianity)">Son of God</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a> (Christ). Christians believe that Jesus, as the Messiah, was <a href="/wiki/Anointing" title="Anointing">anointed</a> by God as savior of humanity, and hold that Jesus' coming was the fulfillment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Messianic_prophecies_of_Jesus" title="Messianic prophecies of Jesus">messianic prophecies</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>. The Christian concept of the Messiah differs significantly from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Messiah" title="Jewish Messiah">the contemporary Jewish concept</a>. The core Christian belief is that through belief in and acceptance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_and_Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Death and Resurrection of Jesus">the death and resurrection of Jesus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">sinful</a> humans can be reconciled to God and thereby are offered salvation and the promise of <a href="/wiki/Immortality" title="Immortality">eternal life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>While there have been many <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">theological</a> disputes over the <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">nature of Jesus</a> over the earliest centuries of Christian history, generally Christians believe that Jesus is <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">God incarnate</a> and "<a href="/wiki/Hypostatic_union" title="Hypostatic union">true God and true man</a>" (or both fully divine and fully human). Jesus, having become <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">fully human</a>, suffered the pains and temptations of a mortal man, but did not <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_sin" title="Christian views on sin">sin</a>. As fully God, he rose to life again. According to the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, he <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">rose</a> from the dead,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus_Christ" title="Ascension of Jesus Christ">ascended</a> to heaven, is seated at the right hand of the Father<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> and will ultimately <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">return</a><sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts+1%3A9%E2%80%9311&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Acts 1:9–11]</a></sup> to fulfill the rest of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Messianic_prophecy" title="Messianic prophecy">Messianic prophecy</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead" title="Resurrection of the dead">resurrection of the dead</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Last Judgment</a> and final establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_God_(Christianity)" title="Kingdom of God (Christianity)">Kingdom of God</a>.</p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">canonical</a> <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">gospels</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Matthew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Luke</a>, Jesus was <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">conceived</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">Holy Spirit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">born</a> from <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">the Virgin Mary</a>. Little of Jesus' childhood is recorded in the canonical gospels, although <a href="/wiki/New_Testament_apocrypha" title="New Testament apocrypha">infancy gospels</a> were popular in antiquity. In comparison, his adulthood, especially the week before his death, is well documented in the gospels contained within the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, because that part of his life is believed to be most important. The biblical accounts of <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus" title="Ministry of Jesus">Jesus' ministry</a> include: <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Jesus" title="Baptism of Jesus">his baptism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miracles_of_Jesus" title="Miracles of Jesus">miracles</a>, preaching, teaching and deeds.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_resurrection">Death and resurrection</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">Crucifixion of Jesus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">Resurrection of Jesus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cristo_crucificado.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3051" data-file-width="2046" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Cristo_crucificado.jpg/200px-Cristo_crucificado.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Cristo_crucificado.jpg/300px-Cristo_crucificado.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Cristo_crucificado.jpg/400px-Cristo_crucificado.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cristo_crucificado.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Christ_Crucified_(Vel%C3%A1zquez)" title="Christ Crucified (Velázquez)">Crucifixion</a></i>, representing the death of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Cross" title="Christian Cross">Cross</a>, painting by <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Velázquez">Diego Velázquez</a>, 17th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Christians consider the resurrection of Jesus to be the cornerstone of their faith (see <a href="/wiki/1_Corinthians_15" title="1 Corinthians 15">1 Corinthians 15</a>) and the most important event in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup> Among Christian beliefs, the death and resurrection of Jesus are two core events on which much of Christian doctrine and theology is based.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACU-Death_.26_Resurrection_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACU-Death_.26_Resurrection-48">[45]</a></sup> According to the New Testament, Jesus was <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion" title="Crucifixion">crucified</a>, died a physical death, was buried within a tomb and rose from the dead three days later.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Jn.+19%3A30%E2%80%9331&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Jn. 19:30–31]</a></sup> <sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mk.+16%3A1&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Mk. 16:1]</a></sup> <sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mark+16%3A6&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[16:6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The New Testament mentions several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resurrection_appearances_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection appearances of Jesus">resurrection appearances of Jesus</a> on different occasions to his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve_apostles" title="Twelve apostles">twelve apostles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Disciple_(Christianity)" title="Disciple (Christianity)">disciples</a>, including "more than five hundred brethren at once",<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1Cor+15%3A6&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[1Cor 15:6]</a></sup> before Jesus' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus_Christ" title="Ascension of Jesus Christ">Ascension</a> to heaven. Jesus' death and resurrection are commemorated by Christians in all worship services, with special emphasis during <a href="/wiki/Holy_Week" title="Holy Week">Holy Week</a> which includes <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday" title="Good Friday">Good Friday</a> and <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a> Sunday.</p>
<p>The death and resurrection of Jesus are usually considered the most important events in <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian theology</a>, partly because they demonstrate that Jesus has power over life and death and therefore has the authority and power to give people <a href="/wiki/Eternal_life_(Christianity)" title="Eternal life (Christianity)">eternal life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Christian churches accept and teach the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> account of the resurrection of Jesus with very few exceptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> Some modern scholars use the belief of Jesus' followers in the resurrection as a point of departure for establishing the continuity of the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">historical Jesus</a> and the proclamation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_church" title="Early church">early church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">liberal Christians</a> do not accept a literal bodily resurrection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> seeing the story as richly symbolic and spiritually nourishing <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">myth</a>. Arguments over death and resurrection claims occur at many religious <a href="/wiki/Debate" title="Debate">debates</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interfaith" title="Interfaith">interfaith dialogues</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a>, an early Christian convert and missionary, wrote, "If Christ was not raised, then all our preaching is useless, and your trust in God is useless."<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1Cor+15%3A14&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[1Cor 15:14]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salvation">Salvation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salvation_(Christianity)" title="Salvation (Christianity)">Salvation (Christianity)</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a>, like Jews and Roman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pagan" title="Pagan">pagans</a> of his time, believed that sacrifice can bring about new kinship ties, purity and eternal life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-remedy_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-remedy-56">[53]</a></sup> For Paul, the necessary sacrifice was the death of Jesus: Gentiles who are "Christ's" are, like Israel, descendants of Abraham and "heirs according to the promise".<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Gal.+3%3A29&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Gal. 3:29]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup> The God who raised Jesus from the dead would also give new life to the "mortal bodies" of Gentile Christians, who had become with Israel the "children of God" and were therefore no longer "in the flesh".<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Rom.+8%3A9%2C11%2C16&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Rom. 8:9,11,16]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-remedy_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-remedy-56">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Modern Christian churches tend to be much more concerned with how humanity can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eternal_salvation" title="Eternal salvation">saved</a> from a universal condition of sin and death than the question of how both Jews and Gentiles can be in God's family. According to both Catholic and Protestant doctrine, salvation comes by Jesus' <a href="/wiki/Substitutionary_atonement" title="Substitutionary atonement">substitutionary death and resurrection</a>. The Catholic Church teaches that salvation does not occur without faithfulness on the part of Christians; converts must live in accordance with principles of love and ordinarily must be baptized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> taught that baptism was necessary for salvation, but modern Lutherans and other Protestants tend to teach that salvation is a gift that comes to an individual by <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace" title="Divine grace">God's grace</a>, sometimes defined as "unmerited favor", even apart from baptism.</p>
<p>Christians differ in their views on the extent to which individuals' salvation is pre-ordained by God. Reformed theology places distinctive emphasis on grace by teaching that individuals are <a href="/wiki/Total_depravity" title="Total depravity">completely incapable of self-redemption</a>, but that <a href="/wiki/Irresistible_grace" title="Irresistible grace">sanctifying grace is irresistible</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup> In contrast Catholics, Orthodox Christians and <a href="/wiki/Arminianism" title="Arminianism">Arminian</a> Protestants believe that the exercise of <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a> is necessary to have faith in Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trinity">Trinity</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="468" data-file-width="520" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/200px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/300px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/400px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> is the belief that <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">God</a> is one God in three persons: the <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">Father</a>, the <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">Son</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>), and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">Holy Spirit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-def-lateran_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-def-lateran-62">[59]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Trinity</i> refers to the teaching that the one God<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monotheism_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monotheism-2">[1]</a></sup> comprises three distinct, eternally co-existing persons; the <i><a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">Father</a></i>, the <i>Son</i> (incarnate in Jesus Christ) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a></i>. Together, these three persons are sometimes called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Godhead_(Christianity)" title="Godhead (Christianity)">Godhead</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup> although there is no single term in use in Scripture to denote the unified Godhead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup> In the words of the <a href="/wiki/Athanasian_Creed" title="Athanasian Creed">Athanasian Creed</a>, an early statement of Christian belief, "the Father is God, the Son is God and the Holy Spirit is God, and yet there are not three Gods but one God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup> They are distinct from another: the Father has no source, the Son is begotten of the Father and the Spirit proceeds from the Father. Though distinct, the three persons cannot be divided from one another in being or in operation. While some Christians also believe that God appeared as the Father in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>, it is agreed that he appeared as the Son in the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, and will still continue to manifest as the Holy Spirit in the present. But still, God still existed as three persons in each of these times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup> However, traditionally there is a belief that it was the Son who appeared in the Old Testament because, for example, when the <a href="/wiki/Trinity#Art" title="Trinity">Trinity is depicted in art</a>, the Son typically has the distinctive appearance, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cruciform_halo" title="Cruciform halo">cruciform halo</a> identifying Christ, and in depictions of the <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">Garden of Eden</a> this looks forward to an Incarnation yet to occur. In some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christian_art" title="Early Christian art">Early Christian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarcophagi" title="Sarcophagi">sarcophagi</a> the Logos is distinguished with a beard, "which allows him to appear ancient, even preexistent."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> is an essential doctrine of mainstream Christianity. From earlier than the times of the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>, 325, Christianity advocated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> the triune <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Mystery" title="Holy Mystery">mystery</a>-nature of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">God</a> as a normative profession of faith. According to <a href="/wiki/Roger_E._Olson" title="Roger E. Olson">Roger E. Olson</a> and Christopher Hall, through prayer, meditation, study and practice, the Christian community concluded "that God must exist as both a unity and trinity", codifying this in ecumenical council at the end of the 4th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to this doctrine, God is not divided in the sense that each person has a third of the whole; rather, each person is considered to be fully God (see <a href="/wiki/Perichoresis" title="Perichoresis">Perichoresis</a>). The distinction lies in their relations, the Father being unbegotten; the Son being begotten of the Father; and the Holy Spirit proceeding from the Father and (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Christian" title="Western Christian">Western Christian</a> theology) <a href="/wiki/Filioque" title="Filioque">from the Son</a>. Regardless of this apparent difference, the three "persons" are each <a href="/wiki/Eternity" title="Eternity">eternal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omnipotent" title="Omnipotent">omnipotent</a>. Other Christian religions including <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mormonism" title="Mormonism">Mormonism</a> and others do not share those views on the Trinity.</p>
<p>The Latin word <i>trias</i>, from which <i>trinity</i> is derived, is first seen in the works of <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_of_Antioch" title="Theophilus of Antioch">Theophilus of Antioch</a>. He wrote of "the Trinity of God (the Father), His Word (the Son) and His Wisdom (Holy Spirit)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup> The term may have been in use before this time. Afterwards it appears in <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> In the following century the word was in general use. It is found in many passages of <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trinitarians">Trinitarians</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinitarianism" title="Trinitarianism">Trinitarianism</a></div>
<p><i>Trinitarianism</i> denotes those Christians who believe in the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>. Almost all Christian denominations and churches hold Trinitarian beliefs. Although the words "Trinity" and "Triune" do not appear in the Bible, theologians beginning in the 3rd century developed the term and concept to facilitate comprehension of the New Testament teachings of God as being Father, Son and Holy Spirit. Since that time, Christian theologians have been careful to emphasize that Trinity does not imply that there are three gods (the antitrinitarian heresy of <a href="/wiki/Tritheism" title="Tritheism">Tritheism</a>), nor that each hypostasis of the Trinity is one-third of an infinite God (partialism), nor that the Son and the Holy Spirit are beings created by and subordinate to the Father (<a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arianism</a>). Rather, the Trinity is defined as one God in three Persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nontrinitarianism">Nontrinitarianism</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nontrinitarianism" title="Nontrinitarianism">Nontrinitarianism</a></div>
<p><i>Nontrinitarianism</i> (or <i>antitrinitarianism</i>) refers to theology that rejects the doctrine of the Trinity. Various nontrinitarian views, such as <a href="/wiki/Adoptionism" title="Adoptionism">adoptionism</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modalism" title="Modalism">modalism</a>, existed in early Christianity, leading to the disputes about <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup> Nontrinitarianism later appeared again in the <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathars" title="Cathars">Cathars</a> in the 11th through 13th centuries, among groups with <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> theology in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> of the 16th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> in the 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> and in some groups arising during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Great_Awakening" title="Second Great Awakening">Second Great Awakening</a> of the 19th century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scriptures">Scriptures</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">Biblical canon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Development_of_the_Christian_Biblical_canon" title="Development of the Christian Biblical canon">Development of the Christian Biblical canon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christian_scripture" title="Christian scripture">Christian scripture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gutenberg_Bible,_Lenox_Copy,_New_York_Public_Library,_2009._Pic_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1920" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Gutenberg_Bible%2C_Lenox_Copy%2C_New_York_Public_Library%2C_2009._Pic_01.jpg/250px-Gutenberg_Bible%2C_Lenox_Copy%2C_New_York_Public_Library%2C_2009._Pic_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Gutenberg_Bible%2C_Lenox_Copy%2C_New_York_Public_Library%2C_2009._Pic_01.jpg/375px-Gutenberg_Bible%2C_Lenox_Copy%2C_New_York_Public_Library%2C_2009._Pic_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Gutenberg_Bible%2C_Lenox_Copy%2C_New_York_Public_Library%2C_2009._Pic_01.jpg/500px-Gutenberg_Bible%2C_Lenox_Copy%2C_New_York_Public_Library%2C_2009._Pic_01.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gutenberg_Bible,_Lenox_Copy,_New_York_Public_Library,_2009._Pic_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> is the sacred book in Christianity.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Christianity, like other religions, has adherents whose beliefs and biblical interpretations vary. Christianity regards the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">biblical canon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, as the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">inspired</a> word of God. The traditional view of inspiration is that God worked through human authors so that what they produced was what God wished to communicate. The Greek word referring to inspiration in <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=2Timothy+3%3A16&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">2 Timothy 3:16</a></span> is <i>theopneustos</i>, which literally means "God-breathed".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some believe that divine inspiration makes our present Bibles <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inerrancy" title="Biblical inerrancy">inerrant</a>. Others claim inerrancy for the Bible in its original manuscripts, although none of those are extant. Still others maintain that only a particular translation is inerrant, such as the <a href="/wiki/King_James_Version" title="King James Version">King James Version</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> Another closely related view is <a href="/wiki/Biblical_infallibility" title="Biblical infallibility">Biblical infallibility</a> or limited inerrancy, which affirms that the Bible is free of error as a guide to salvation, but may include errors on matters such as history, geography or science.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Books_of_the_Bible" title="Books of the Bible">books of the Bible</a> accepted by the Orthodox, Catholic and Protestant churches vary somewhat, with Jews accepting only the Hebrew Bible as canonical; there is however substantial overlap. These variations are a reflection of the range of <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">traditions</a>, and of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_council" title="Ecumenical council">councils</a> that have convened on the subject. Every version of the Old Testament always includes the books of the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>, the canon of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>. The Catholic and Orthodox canons, in addition to the Tanakh, also include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuterocanonical_Books" title="Deuterocanonical Books">Deuterocanonical Books</a> as part of the Old Testament. These books appear in the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a>, but are regarded by Protestants to be <a href="/wiki/Biblical_apocrypha" title="Biblical apocrypha">apocryphal</a>. However, they are considered to be important historical documents which help to inform the understanding of words, grammar and syntax used in the historical period of their conception. Some versions of the Bible include a separate Apocrypha section between the Old Testament and the New Testament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> The New Testament, originally written in <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>, contains 27 books which are agreed upon by all churches.</p>
<p>Modern scholarship has raised many issues with the Bible. While the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" title="Authorized King James Version">Authorized King James Version</a> is held to by many because of its striking English prose, in fact it was translated from the Erasmus Greek Bible which in turn "was based on a single 12th Century manuscript that is one of the worst manuscripts we have available to us".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrman209-183_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman209-183-85">[82]</a></sup> Much scholarship in the past several hundred years has gone into comparing different manuscripts in order to reconstruct the original text. Another issue is that several books are considered to be forgeries. The injunction that women "be silent and submissive" in 1 Timothy 2<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> is thought by many to be a forgery by a follower of Paul, a similar phrase in 1 Corinthians 14,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> which is thought to be by Paul, appears in different places in different manuscripts and is thought to originally be a margin note by a copyist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrman209-183_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman209-183-85">[82]</a></sup> Other verses in 1 Corinthians, such as 1 Corinthians 11:2–16 where women are instructed to wear a covering over their hair "when they pray or prophesies",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup> contradict this verse.</p>
<p>A final issue with the Bible is the way in which books were selected for inclusion in the New Testament. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gnostic_Gospels" title="Gnostic Gospels">Other Gospels</a> have now been recovered, such as those found near <a href="/wiki/Nag_Hammadi" title="Nag Hammadi">Nag Hammadi</a> in 1945, and while some of these texts are quite different from what Christians have been used to, it should be understood that some of this newly recovered Gospel material is quite possibly contemporaneous with, or even earlier than, the New Testament Gospels. The core of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Thomas" title="Gospel of Thomas">Gospel of Thomas</a>, in particular, may date from as early as AD 50 (although some major scholars contest this early dating),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup> and if so would provide an insight into the earliest gospel texts that underlie the canonical Gospels, texts that are mentioned in Luke 1:1–2. The Gospel of Thomas contains much that is familiar from the canonical Gospels—verse 113, for example ("The Father's Kingdom is spread out upon the earth, but people do not see it"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup> is reminiscent of Luke 17:20–21<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup>—and the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>, with a terminology and approach that is suggestive of what was later termed <i>Gnosticism</i>, has recently been seen as a possible response to the Gospel of Thomas, a text that is commonly labelled <i>proto-Gnostic</i>. Scholarship, then, is currently exploring the relationship in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Church</a> between mystical speculation and experience on the one hand and the search for church order on the other, by analyzing new-found texts, by subjecting canonical texts to further scrutiny, and by an examination of the passage of New Testament texts to canonical status.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_interpretation">Catholic interpretation</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_of_saint_Peter_basilica_from_a_roof.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/View_of_saint_Peter_basilica_from_a_roof.jpg/250px-View_of_saint_Peter_basilica_from_a_roof.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/View_of_saint_Peter_basilica_from_a_roof.jpg/375px-View_of_saint_Peter_basilica_from_a_roof.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/View_of_saint_Peter_basilica_from_a_roof.jpg/500px-View_of_saint_Peter_basilica_from_a_roof.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:View_of_saint_Peter_basilica_from_a_roof.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter's Basilica">St. Peter's Basilica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a>, the largest church in the world and a symbol of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_theology_of_Scripture" title="Roman Catholic theology of Scripture">Catholic theology of Scripture</a></div>
<p>In antiquity, two schools of exegesis developed in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/School_of_Antioch" title="School of Antioch">Antioch</a>. Alexandrine interpretation, exemplified by <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a>, tended to read Scripture <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegorically</a>, while Antiochene interpretation adhered to the literal sense, holding that other meanings (called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theoria" title="Theoria">theoria</a></i>) could only be accepted if based on the literal meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> theology distinguishes two senses of scripture: the literal and the spiritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>literal</i> sense of understanding scripture is the meaning conveyed by the words of Scripture. The <i>spiritual</i> sense is further subdivided into:</p>
<ul>
<li>The <i>allegorical</i> sense, which includes <a href="/wiki/Typology_(theology)" title="Typology (theology)">typology</a>. An example would be the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passage_of_the_Red_Sea" title="Passage of the Red Sea">parting of the Red Sea</a> being understood as a "type" (sign) of baptism.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1Cor+10%3A2&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[1Cor 10:2]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Moral" title="Moral">moral</a></i> sense, which understands the scripture to contain some ethical teaching.</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Anagoge" title="Anagoge">anagogical</a></i> sense, which applies to <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">eschatology</a>, eternity and the <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse" title="Apocalypse">consummation of the world</a></li>
</ul>
<p>Regarding <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a>, following the rules of sound interpretation, Catholic theology holds:</p>
<ul>
<li>The injunction that all other senses of sacred scripture are based on the <i>literal</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li>That the historicity of the Gospels must be absolutely and constantly held<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li>That scripture must be read within the "living Tradition of the whole Church"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup> and</li>
<li>That "the task of interpretation has been entrusted to the bishops in communion with the successor of Peter, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">Bishop</a> of <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Rome" title="Diocese of Rome">Rome</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_interpretation">Protestant interpretation</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther,_1528_(Veste_Coburg)_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2363" data-file-width="1999" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/230px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/345px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/460px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther%2C_1528_%28Veste_Coburg%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Martin_Luther,_1528_(Veste_Coburg)_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestants" title="Protestants">Protestants</a> believe <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>'s basic beliefs against the Catholic Church: <a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">Sola scriptura</a> (by Scripture alone), <a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">Sola fide</a> (by faith alone), <a href="/wiki/Sola_gratia" title="Sola gratia">Sola gratia</a> (by grace alone), <a href="/wiki/Solus_Christus" title="Solus Christus">Solus Christus</a> (through Christ alone) and <a href="/wiki/Soli_Deo_gloria" title="Soli Deo gloria">Soli Deo gloria</a> (glory to God alone).</div>
</div>
</div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Clarity_of_Scripture">Clarity of Scripture</span></h5>
<dl>
<dd>Protestant Christians believe that the Bible is a self-sufficient revelation, the final authority on all Christian doctrine, and <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revealed all truth</a> necessary for salvation. This concept is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">sola scriptura</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup> Protestants characteristically believe that ordinary believers may reach an adequate understanding of Scripture because Scripture itself is clear (or "perspicuous"), because of the help of the Holy Spirit, or both. Martin Luther believed that without God's help Scripture would be "enveloped in darkness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foutz_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foutz-101">[98]</a></sup> He advocated "one definite and simple understanding of Scripture".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foutz_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foutz-101">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> wrote, "all who refuse not to follow the Holy Spirit as their guide, find in the Scripture a clear light."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup> The Second <a href="/wiki/Helvetic_Republic" title="Helvetic Republic">Helvetic</a> Confession, composed by the pastor of the Reformed church in Zürich (successor to Protestant reformer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zwingli" title="Zwingli">Zwingli</a>) was adopted as a declaration of doctrine by most European Reformed churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_intended_meaning_of_Scripture">Original intended meaning of Scripture</span></h5>
<dl>
<dd>Protestants stress the meaning conveyed by the words of Scripture, the <a href="/wiki/Historical-grammatical_method" title="Historical-grammatical method">historical-grammatical method</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup> The historical-grammatical method or grammatico-historical method is an effort in <a href="/wiki/Biblical_hermeneutics" title="Biblical hermeneutics">Biblical hermeneutics</a> to find the intended original meaning in the text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elwell565_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elwell565-105">[102]</a></sup> This original intended meaning of the text is drawn out through examination of the passage in light of the grammatical and syntactical aspects, the historical background, the literary genre as well as theological (canonical) considerations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup> The historical-grammatical method distinguishes between the one original meaning and the significance of the text. The significance of the text includes the ensuing use of the text or application. The original passage is seen as having only a single meaning or sense. As Milton S. Terry said: "A fundamental principle in grammatico-historical exposition is that the words and sentences can have but one significance in one and the same connection. The moment we neglect this principle we drift out upon a sea of uncertainty and conjecture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup> Technically speaking, the grammatical-historical method of interpretation is distinct from the determination of the passage's significance in light of that interpretation. Taken together, both define the term (Biblical) hermeneutics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elwell565_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elwell565-105">[102]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Some Protestant interpreters make use of typology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eschatology">Eschatology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">Christian eschatology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kohrvirab.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2334" data-file-width="3504" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Kohrvirab.jpg/250px-Kohrvirab.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Kohrvirab.jpg/375px-Kohrvirab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Kohrvirab.jpg/500px-Kohrvirab.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kohrvirab.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 7th-century <a href="/wiki/Khor_Virap" title="Khor Virap">Khor Virap</a> monastery in the shadow of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ararat" title="Mount Ararat">Mount Ararat</a>. <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> was the first state to adopt Christianity as the state religion, in AD 301.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armenia_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Armenia-109">[106]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The end of things, whether the end of an individual life, the end of the age, or the end of the world, broadly speaking is Christian eschatology; the study of the destiny of humans as it is revealed in the Bible. The major issues in Christian eschatology are the <a href="/wiki/Great_Tribulation" title="Great Tribulation">Tribulation</a>, death and the afterlife, the <a href="/wiki/Rapture" title="Rapture">Rapture</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">Second Coming</a> of Jesus, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_Dead" title="Resurrection of the Dead">Resurrection of the Dead</a>, Heaven and Hell, <a href="/wiki/Millennialism" title="Millennialism">Millennialism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Last Judgment</a>, the end of the world and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Heavens_and_New_Earth" title="New Heavens and New Earth">New Heavens and New Earth</a>.</p>
<p>Christians believe that the second coming of Christ will occur at the <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">end of time</a> after a period of severe persecution (the Great Tribulation). All who have died will be <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead" title="Resurrection of the dead">resurrected bodily from the dead</a> for the Last Judgment. Jesus will fully establish the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_God" title="Kingdom of God">Kingdom of God</a> in fulfillment of <a href="/wiki/Bible_prophecy" title="Bible prophecy">scriptural prophecies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-res_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-res-110">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_afterlife">Death and afterlife</span></h4>
<p>Most Christians believe that human beings experience divine judgment and are rewarded either with eternal life or <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">eternal damnation</a>. This includes the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">general judgement</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead" title="Resurrection of the dead">resurrection of the dead</a> as well as the belief (held by Roman Catholics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CEParticularJudgement_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-CEParticularJudgement-112">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ott_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ott-113">[110]</a></sup> Orthodox<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup> and most Protestants) in a <a href="/wiki/Particular_judgment" title="Particular judgment">judgment particular to the individual soul</a> upon physical death.</p>
<p>In Roman Catholicism, those who die in a state of grace, i.e., without any mortal sin separating them from God, but are still imperfectly purified from the effects of sin, undergo purification through the intermediate state of <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">purgatory</a> to achieve the holiness necessary for entrance into God's presence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup> Those who have attained this goal are called <i>saints</i> (Latin <i>sanctus</i>, "holy").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some Christian groups, such as Seventh-day Adventists, hold to <a href="/wiki/Christian_mortalism" title="Christian mortalism">mortalism</a>, the belief that the human soul is not naturally immortal, and is unconscious during the intermediate state between bodily death and resurrection. These Christians also hold to <a href="/wiki/Annihilationism" title="Annihilationism">Annihilationism</a>, the belief that subsequent to the final judgement, the wicked will cease to exist rather than suffer everlasting torment. Jehovah's Witnesses hold to a similar view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship">Worship</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christian_worship" title="Christian worship">Christian worship</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">Mass (liturgy)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reformed_worship" title="Reformed worship">Reformed worship</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_worship" title="Contemporary worship">Contemporary worship</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thebible33.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Thebible33.jpg/230px-Thebible33.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Thebible33.jpg/345px-Thebible33.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Thebible33.jpg/460px-Thebible33.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thebible33.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Samples of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> religious objects—the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Crucifix" title="Crucifix">crucifix</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Rosary" title="Rosary">rosary</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Justin_Martyr" title="Justin Martyr">Justin Martyr</a> described 2nd-century Christian <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a> in his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Apology" title="First Apology">First Apology</a></i> (c. 150) to Emperor <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a>, and his description remains relevant to the basic structure of Christian liturgical worship:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>And on the day called Sunday, all who live in cities or in the country gather together to one place, and the memoirs of the apostles or the writings of the prophets are read, as long as time permits; then, when the reader has ceased, the <a href="/wiki/Presbyter" title="Presbyter">president</a> verbally instructs, and exhorts to the imitation of these good things. Then we all rise together and pray, and, as we before said, when our prayer is ended, bread and wine and water are brought, and the president in like manner offers prayers and thanksgivings, according to his ability, and the people assent, saying <a href="/wiki/Amen" title="Amen">Amen</a>; and there is a distribution to each, and a participation of that over which thanks have been given, and to those who are absent a portion is sent by the deacons. And they who are well to do, and willing, give what each thinks fit; and what is collected is deposited with the president, who succours the orphans and widows and those who, through sickness or any other cause, are in want, and those who are in bonds and the strangers sojourning among us, and in a word takes care of all who are in need.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-justin_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-justin-119">[116]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Thus, as Justin described, Christians assemble for communal worship on Sunday, the day of the resurrection, though other liturgical practices often occur outside this setting. Scripture readings are drawn from the Old and New Testaments, but especially the gospel accounts. Often these are arranged on an <a href="/wiki/Annual_cycle" title="Annual cycle">annual cycle</a>, using a book called a <a href="/wiki/Lectionary" title="Lectionary">lectionary</a>. Instruction is given based on these readings, called a <a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">sermon</a>, or homily. There are a variety of congregational <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayers</a>, including thanksgiving, confession and intercession, which occur throughout the service and take a variety of forms including recited, responsive, silent, or sung. The <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord's Prayer">Lord's Prayer</a>, or Our Father, is regularly prayed.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Worship-team.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="965" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Worship-team.jpg/250px-Worship-team.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Worship-team.jpg/375px-Worship-team.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Worship-team.jpg/500px-Worship-team.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Worship-team.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A modern Protestant worship band leading a contemporary worship session</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some groups depart from this traditional liturgical structure. A division is often made between "<a href="/wiki/High_church" title="High church">High</a>" <a href="/wiki/Church_service" title="Church service">church services</a>, characterized by greater solemnity and ritual, and "<a href="/wiki/Low_church" title="Low church">Low</a>" services, but even within these two categories there is great diversity in forms of worship. <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist_Church" title="Seventh-day Adventist Church">Seventh-day Adventists</a> meet on Saturday, while others do not meet on a weekly basis. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charismatic_movement" title="Charismatic movement">Charismatic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentecostal" title="Pentecostal">Pentecostal</a> congregations may spontaneously feel led by the Holy Spirit to action rather than follow a formal order of service, including spontaneous prayer. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_of_Friends" title="Society of Friends">Quakers</a> sit quietly until moved by the Holy Spirit to speak.</p>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">evangelical</a> services resemble concerts with <a href="/wiki/Christian_rock" title="Christian rock">rock and pop music</a>, dancing and use of multimedia. For groups which do not recognize a priesthood distinct from ordinary believers the services are generally led by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_of_religion" title="Minister of religion">minister</a>, <a href="/wiki/Preacher" title="Preacher">preacher</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Pastor" title="Pastor">pastor</a>. Still others may lack any formal leaders, either in principle or by local necessity. Some churches use only <a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a cappella</a> music, either on principle (for example, many <a href="/wiki/Churches_of_Christ" title="Churches of Christ">Churches of Christ</a> object to the use of instruments in worship) or by tradition (as in Orthodoxy).</p>
<p>Nearly all forms of <a href="/wiki/Churchmanship" title="Churchmanship">churchmanship</a> celebrate the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> (Holy Communion), which consists of a consecrated meal. It is reenacted in accordance with Jesus' instruction at the Last Supper that his followers do in remembrance of him as when he gave his disciples <a href="/wiki/Sacramental_bread" title="Sacramental bread">bread</a>, saying, "This is my body", and gave them <a href="/wiki/Sacramental_wine" title="Sacramental wine">wine</a> saying, "This is my blood".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup> Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_denominations" title="Christian denominations">Christian denominations</a> practice <a href="/wiki/Closed_communion" title="Closed communion">closed communion</a>. They offer communion to those who are already united in that denomination or sometimes individual church. Catholics restrict participation to their members who are not in a state of <a href="/wiki/Mortal_sin" title="Mortal sin">mortal sin</a>. Most other churches practice <a href="/wiki/Open_communion" title="Open communion">open communion</a> since they view communion as a means to unity, rather than an end, and invite all believing Christians to participate.</p>
<p>Worship can be varied for special events like <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptisms</a> or weddings in the service or significant <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints" title="Calendar of saints">feast days</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early church</a>, Christians and those yet to complete initiation would separate for the Eucharistic part of the worship. In many churches today, adults and children will separate for all or some of the service to receive age-appropriate teaching. Such children's worship is often called <a href="/wiki/Sunday_school" title="Sunday school">Sunday school</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sabbath_school" title="Sabbath school">Sabbath school</a> (Sunday schools are often held before rather than during services).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sacraments">Sacraments</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">Sacrament</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sacraments_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Sacraments of the Catholic Church">Sacraments of the Catholic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglican_sacraments" title="Anglican sacraments">Anglican sacraments</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_sacraments" title="Lutheran sacraments">Lutheran sacraments</a></div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #BCF5A9;">
<div class="" style="background-color: #BCF5A9; color: black; text-align: center; font-size: larger; font-weight: bold;">2nd-century description of the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a></div>
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>And this food is called among us <i>Eukaristia</i> [the Eucharist], of which no one is allowed to partake but the man who believes that the things which we teach are true, and who has been washed with the washing that is for the remission of sins, and unto regeneration, and who is so living as Christ has enjoined. For not as common bread and common drink do we receive these; but in like manner as Jesus Christ our Savior, having been made flesh by the Word of God, had both flesh and blood for our salvation, so likewise have we been taught that the food which is blessed by the prayer of His word, and from which our blood and flesh by transmutation are nourished, is the flesh and blood of that Jesus who was made flesh.</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><a href="/wiki/Justin_Martyr" title="Justin Martyr">Justin Martyr</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-justin_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-justin-119">[116]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>In Christian belief and practice, a <i>sacrament</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Rite" title="Rite">rite</a>, instituted by Christ, that confers <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace" title="Divine grace">grace</a>, constituting a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred_Mysteries" title="Sacred Mysteries">sacred mystery</a>. The term is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>sacramentum</i>, which was used to translate the Greek word for <i>mystery</i>. Views concerning both which rites are sacramental, and what it means for an act to be a sacrament, vary among Christian denominations and traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODCC1435_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC1435-121">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most conventional functional definition of a sacrament is that it is an outward sign, instituted by Christ, that conveys an inward, spiritual grace through Christ. The two most widely accepted sacraments are <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> (or Holy Communion), however, the majority of Christians also recognize five additional sacraments: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confirmation_(Christian_sacrament)" title="Confirmation (Christian sacrament)">Confirmation</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chrismation" title="Chrismation">Chrismation</a> in the Orthodox tradition), <a href="/wiki/Holy_orders" title="Holy orders">Holy orders</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ordination" title="Ordination">ordination</a>), <a href="/wiki/Penance" title="Penance">Penance</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Confession_(religion)" title="Confession (religion)">Confession</a>), <a href="/wiki/Anointing_of_the_Sick" title="Anointing of the Sick">Anointing of the Sick</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matrimony" title="Matrimony">Matrimony</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_marriage" title="Christian views on marriage">Christian views on marriage</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODCC1435_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC1435-121">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Taken together, these are the <a href="/wiki/Sacraments_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Sacraments of the Catholic Church">Seven Sacraments</a> as recognized by churches in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Church" title="High Church">High Church</a> tradition—notably <a href="/wiki/Sacraments_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Sacraments of the Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church" title="Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodox" title="Oriental Orthodox">Oriental Orthodox</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_Catholic_Churches" title="Independent Catholic Churches">Independent Catholic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Catholic" title="Old Catholic">Old Catholic</a>, many <a href="/wiki/Anglican_sacraments" title="Anglican sacraments">Anglicans</a> and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutherans" title="Lutherans">Lutherans</a>. Most other denominations and traditions typically affirm only Baptism and Eucharist as sacraments, while some Protestant groups, such as the Quakers, reject sacramental theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODCC1435_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC1435-121">[118]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_denominations" title="Christian denominations">Christian denominations</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a>, which believe these rites do not communicate grace, prefer to call Baptism and Holy Communion <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinance_(Christian)" title="Ordinance (Christian)">ordinances</a></i> rather than sacraments.</p>
<p>In addition to this, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> has two additional sacraments in place of the traditional sacraments of Matrimony and the Anointing of the Sick. These include <a href="/wiki/Holy_Leaven" title="Holy Leaven">Holy Leaven</a> (Melka) and the <a href="/wiki/Sign_of_the_cross" title="Sign of the cross">sign of the cross</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holy_Apostolic_Catholic_Assyrian_Church_of_the_East.2C_Archdiocese_of_Australia.2C_New_Zealand_and_Lebanon_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holy_Apostolic_Catholic_Assyrian_Church_of_the_East.2C_Archdiocese_of_Australia.2C_New_Zealand_and_Lebanon-122">[119]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skondals_kyrka_2005.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="776" data-file-width="928" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Skondals_kyrka_2005.jpg/179px-Skondals_kyrka_2005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Skondals_kyrka_2005.jpg/269px-Skondals_kyrka_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Skondals_kyrka_2005.jpg/359px-Skondals_kyrka_2005.jpg 2x" width="179"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a>, specifically <a href="/wiki/Infant_baptism" title="Infant baptism">infant baptism</a>, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> tradition</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liturgical_calendar">Liturgical calendar</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_year" title="Liturgical year">Liturgical year</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints" title="Calendar of saints">Calendar of saints</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nativity_tree2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Nativity_tree2011.jpg/220px-Nativity_tree2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Nativity_tree2011.jpg/330px-Nativity_tree2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Nativity_tree2011.jpg/440px-Nativity_tree2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nativity_tree2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Nativity_scene" title="Nativity scene">depiction of the Nativity</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Christmas_tree" title="Christmas tree">Christmas tree</a> backdrop</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Roman Catholics, Anglicans, Eastern Christians and traditional Protestant communities frame worship around the <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_year" title="Liturgical year">liturgical year</a>. The liturgical cycle divides the year into a series of <a href="/wiki/Season" title="Season">seasons</a>, each with their theological emphases, and modes of prayer, which can be signified by different ways of decorating churches, colours of <a href="/wiki/Parament" title="Parament">paraments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vestment" title="Vestment">vestments</a> for clergy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calendar_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-calendar-123">[120]</a></sup> scriptural readings, themes for preaching and even different traditions and practices often observed personally or in the home.</p>
