<p>Western Christian liturgical calendars are based on the cycle of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rite" title="Roman Rite">Roman Rite</a> of the Catholic Church,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calendar_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-calendar-123">[120]</a></sup> and Eastern Christians use analogous calendars based on the cycle of their respective <a href="/wiki/Rite#Christian" title="Rite">rites</a>. Calendars set aside holy days, such as <a href="/wiki/Solemnity" title="Solemnity">solemnities</a> which commemorate an event in the life of Jesus, Mary or the <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>, and periods of <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a> and other pious events such as <a href="/wiki/Memoria" title="Memoria">memoria</a> or lesser festivals commemorating saints. Christian groups that do not follow a liturgical tradition often retain certain celebrations, such as <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Pentecost</a>: these are the celebrations of Christ's birth, resurrection and the descent of the Holy Spirit upon the Church, respectively. A few denominations make no use of a liturgical calendar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">Symbols</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christian_symbolism" title="Christian symbolism">Christian symbolism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nideggen-St.Johannes_Baptist249.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2520" data-file-width="3776" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Nideggen-St.Johannes_Baptist249.JPG/250px-Nideggen-St.Johannes_Baptist249.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Nideggen-St.Johannes_Baptist249.JPG/375px-Nideggen-St.Johannes_Baptist249.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Nideggen-St.Johannes_Baptist249.JPG/500px-Nideggen-St.Johannes_Baptist249.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nideggen-St.Johannes_Baptist249.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">cross</a> and the fish are two common symbols of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>. The letters of the Greek word ΙΧΘΥΣ <a href="/wiki/Ichthys" title="Ichthys">Ichthys</a> (fish) form an acronym for "Ίησοῦς Χριστός, Θεοῦ Υἱός, Σωτήρ", which translates into English as "Jesus Christ, God's Son, Savior".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Christianity has not generally practiced <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Christianity" title="Aniconism in Christianity">aniconism</a>, or the avoidance or prohibition of types of images, even if the early <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Christian" title="Jewish Christian">Jewish Christians</a> sects, as well as some modern <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">denominations</a>, preferred to some extent not to use figures in their symbols, by invoking the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Decalogue's</a> prohibition of <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolatry</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">cross</a>, which is today one of the most widely recognized symbols in the world, was used as a Christian symbol from the earliest times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup> Tertullian, in his book <i>De Corona</i>, tells how it was already a tradition for Christians to trace repeatedly on their foreheads the sign of the cross.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[124]</a></sup> Although the cross was known to the early Christians, the <a href="/wiki/Crucifix" title="Crucifix">crucifix</a> did not appear in use until the 5th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soc_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-soc-128">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the symbols employed by the primitive Christians, that of the fish or <a href="/wiki/Ichthys" title="Ichthys">Ichthys</a> seems to have ranked first in importance. From monumental sources such as tombs it is known that the symbolic fish was familiar to Christians from the earliest times. The fish was depicted as a Christian symbol in the first decades of the 2nd century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cefish_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-cefish-129">[126]</a></sup> Its popularity among Christians was due principally, it would seem, to the famous acrostic consisting of the initial letters of five Greek words forming the word for fish (Ichthys), which words briefly but clearly described the character of Christ and the claim to worship of believers: <i>Iesous Christos Theou Yios Soter</i> (Ίησοῦς Χριστός, Θεοῦ Υἱός, Σωτήρ), meaning, <i>Jesus Christ, Son of God, Savior</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cefish_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-cefish-129">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other major Christian symbols include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chi-Rho" title="Chi-Rho">chi-rho monogram</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dove" title="Dove">dove</a> (symbolic of the Holy Spirit), the sacrificial lamb (symbolic of Christ's sacrifice), the <a href="/wiki/Vine" title="Vine">vine</a> (symbolizing the necessary connectedness of the Christian with Christ) and many others. These all derive from writings found in the New Testament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soc_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-soc-128">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baptism">Baptism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Almeida_J%C3%BAnior_-_Batismo_de_Jesus,_1895.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1559" data-file-width="1012" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Almeida_J%C3%BAnior_-_Batismo_de_Jesus%2C_1895.JPG/200px-Almeida_J%C3%BAnior_-_Batismo_de_Jesus%2C_1895.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Almeida_J%C3%BAnior_-_Batismo_de_Jesus%2C_1895.JPG/300px-Almeida_J%C3%BAnior_-_Batismo_de_Jesus%2C_1895.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Almeida_J%C3%BAnior_-_Batismo_de_Jesus%2C_1895.JPG/400px-Almeida_J%C3%BAnior_-_Batismo_de_Jesus%2C_1895.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Almeida_J%C3%BAnior_-_Batismo_de_Jesus,_1895.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The baptism of Jesus depicted by <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Ferraz_de_Almeida_J%C3%BAnior" title="José Ferraz de Almeida Júnior">Almeida Júnior</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Baptism is the ritual act, with the use of water, by which a person is admitted to membership of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">Church</a>. Beliefs on baptism vary among denominations. Differences occur firstly on whether the act has any spiritual significance. Some, such as the Catholic and Eastern Orthodox churches, as well as Lutherans and Anglicans, hold to the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Baptismal_regeneration" title="Baptismal regeneration">baptismal regeneration</a>, which affirms that baptism creates or strengthens a person's faith, and is intimately linked to salvation. Others view baptism as a purely symbolic act, an external public declaration of the inward change which has taken place in the person, but not as spiritually efficacious. Secondly, there are differences of opinion on the methodology of the act. These methods are: by <a href="/wiki/Immersion_baptism" title="Immersion baptism"><i>immersion</i></a>; if immersion is total, by <i>submersion</i>; by <a href="/wiki/Affusion" title="Affusion">affusion</a> (pouring); and by <a href="/wiki/Aspersion" title="Aspersion">aspersion</a> (sprinkling). Those who hold the first view may also adhere to the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Infant_baptism" title="Infant baptism">infant baptism</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[127]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church" title="Orthodox Church">Orthodox Churches</a> all practice infant baptism and always baptize by total immersion repeated three times in the name of the Father, the Son and the Holy Spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup> The Catholic Church also practices infant baptism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup> usually by affusion, and utilizing the <a href="/wiki/Trinitarian_formula" title="Trinitarian formula">Trinitarian formula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prayer">Prayer</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prayer_in_Christianity" title="Prayer in Christianity">Prayer in Christianity</a></div>
<p>Jesus' teaching on prayer in the <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a> displays a distinct lack of interest in the external aspects of prayer. A concern with the techniques of prayer is condemned as 'pagan', and instead a simple trust in God's fatherly goodness is encouraged.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mat.+6%3A5%E2%80%9315&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Mat. 6:5–15]</a></sup> Elsewhere in the New Testament this same freedom of access to God is also emphasized.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Phil.+4%3A6&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Phil. 4:6]</a></sup><sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Jam.+5%3A13%E2%80%9319&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Jam. 5:13–19]</a></sup> This confident position should be understood in light of Christian belief in the unique relationship between the believer and Christ through the indwelling of the Holy Spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBDprayer_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-NBDprayer-135">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In subsequent Christian traditions, certain physical gestures are emphasized, including medieval gestures such as <a href="/wiki/Genuflection" title="Genuflection">genuflection</a> or making the <a href="/wiki/Sign_of_the_cross" title="Sign of the cross">sign of the cross</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kneeling" title="Kneeling">Kneeling</a>, bowing and <a href="/wiki/Prostration" title="Prostration">prostrations</a> (see also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poklon" title="Poklon">poklon</a>) are often practiced in more traditional branches of Christianity. Frequently in Western Christianity the hands are placed palms together and forward as in the feudal <a href="/wiki/Commendation_ceremony" title="Commendation ceremony">commendation ceremony</a>. At other times the older <a href="/wiki/Orans" title="Orans">orans</a> posture may be used, with palms up and elbows in.</p>
<p><i>Intercessory prayer</i> is prayer offered for the benefit of other people. There are many intercessory prayers recorded in the Bible, including prayers of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostle_Peter" title="Apostle Peter">Apostle Peter</a> on behalf of sick persons<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts+9%3A40&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Acts 9:40]</a></sup> and by <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophets</a> of the Old Testament in favor of other people.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1Ki+17%3A19%E2%80%9322&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[1Ki 17:19–22]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">Epistle of James</a>, no distinction is made between the intercessory prayer offered by ordinary believers and the prominent Old Testament prophet <a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Elijah</a>.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Jam+5%3A16%E2%80%9318&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Jam 5:16–18]</a></sup> The effectiveness of prayer in Christianity derives from the power of God rather than the status of the one praying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBDprayer_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-NBDprayer-135">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ancient church, in both <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">Western Christianity</a>, developed a tradition of asking for the <a href="/wiki/Intercession_of_saints" title="Intercession of saints">intercession of (deceased) saints</a>, and this remains the practice of most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodox" title="Oriental Orthodox">Oriental Orthodox</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a>, and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> churches. Churches of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, however, rejected prayer to the saints, largely on the basis of the sole mediatorship of Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup> The reformer <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a> admitted that he had offered prayers to the saints until his reading of the Bible convinced him that this was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idolatry_in_Christianity" title="Idolatry in Christianity">idolatrous</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Catechism_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Catechism of the Catholic Church">Catechism of the Catholic Church</a>: "Prayer is the raising of one's mind and heart to God or the requesting of good things from God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> in the Anglican tradition is a guide which provides a set order for church services, containing set prayers, scripture readings, and hymns or sung Psalms.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity" title="History of Christianity">History of Christianity</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Church_and_Christological_Councils">Early Church and Christological Councils</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_Christianity" title="Origins of Christianity">Origins of Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christianity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/First_seven_Ecumenical_Councils" title="First seven Ecumenical Councils">First seven Ecumenical Councils</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inside_of_Saint_Ananias.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Inside_of_Saint_Ananias.jpg/230px-Inside_of_Saint_Ananias.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Inside_of_Saint_Ananias.jpg/345px-Inside_of_Saint_Ananias.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Inside_of_Saint_Ananias.jpg/460px-Inside_of_Saint_Ananias.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inside_of_Saint_Ananias.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/House_of_Saint_Ananias" title="House of Saint Ananias">Chapel of Saint Ananias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, an early example of a Christian house of worship; built in the 1st century AD</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="1276" height="92" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg/230px-Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg/345px-Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg/460px-Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ephesus_IchthysCrop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An early circular <a href="/wiki/Ichthys" title="Ichthys">ichthys</a> symbol, created by combining the Greek letters <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%99%CE%A7%CE%98%CE%A5%CE%A3" title="ΙΧΘΥΣ">ΙΧΘΥΣ</a> into a wheel. <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, Asia Minor.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kadisha_Valley_cross.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1821" data-file-width="3137" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Kadisha_Valley_cross.jpg/230px-Kadisha_Valley_cross.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Kadisha_Valley_cross.jpg/345px-Kadisha_Valley_cross.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Kadisha_Valley_cross.jpg/460px-Kadisha_Valley_cross.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kadisha_Valley_cross.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Kadisha_Valley" title="Kadisha Valley">Kadisha Valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, home to some of the earliest Christian monasteries in the world</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Christianity began as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> sect in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> of the middle east in the mid-1st century. Other than <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple_Judaism" title="Second Temple Judaism">Second Temple Judaism</a>, the primary religious influences of <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christianity</a> are <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-name_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-name-8">[note 2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esler_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Esler-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[136]</a></sup> John Bowker states that Christian ideas such as "angels, the end of the world, a final judgment, the resurrection and heaven and hell received form and substance from ... Zoroastrian beliefs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup> Its earliest development took place under the leadership of the remaining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles" title="Twelve Apostles">Twelve Apostles</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Saint Peter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a>, followed by the early <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a>, whom Christians consider the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_succession" title="Apostolic succession">successors of the Apostles</a>.</p>
<p>According to the Christian scriptures, Christians were from the beginning subject to <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians" title="Persecution of Christians">persecution</a> by some Jewish and Roman religious authorities, who disagreed with the apostles' teachings (See <a href="/wiki/Split_of_early_Christianity_and_Judaism" title="Split of early Christianity and Judaism">Split of early Christianity and Judaism</a>). This involved punishments, including death, for Christians such as <a href="/wiki/Stephen" title="Stephen">Stephen</a><sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts+7%3A59&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Acts 7:59]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/James,_son_of_Zebedee" title="James, son of Zebedee">James, son of Zebedee</a>.<sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts+12%3A2&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Acts 12:2]</a></sup> Larger-scale persecutions followed at the hands of the authorities of the Roman Empire, first in the year 64, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Nero" title="Emperor Nero">Emperor Nero</a> blamed them for the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Rome" title="Great Fire of Rome">Great Fire of Rome</a>. According to Church tradition, it was under Nero's persecution that early Church leaders Peter and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul of Tarsus</a> were each martyred in Rome.</p>
<p>Further widespread <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persecution_of_early_Christians_by_the_Romans" title="Persecution of early Christians by the Romans">persecutions</a> of the Church occurred under nine subsequent Roman emperors, most intensely under <a href="/wiki/Decius" title="Decius">Decius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a>. From the year 150, Christian teachers began to produce theological and apologetic works aimed at defending the faith. These authors are known as the <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a>, and study of them is called <a href="/wiki/Patristics" title="Patristics">Patristics</a>. Notable early Fathers include <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_of_Antioch" title="Ignatius of Antioch">Ignatius of Antioch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polycarp" title="Polycarp">Polycarp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justin_Martyr" title="Justin Martyr">Justin Martyr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a>. However, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> is considered the first nation to accept Christianity in AD 301.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armenia_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Armenia-109">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-141">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-almanac_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-almanac-142">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>King Trdat IV made Christianity the state religion in Armenia between 301 and 314, it was not an entirely new religion in Armenia. It penetrated into the country from at least the third century, but may have been present even earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maarten_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-maarten-143">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span id="End_of_Roman_persecution_under_Emperor_Constantine_(AD_313)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_Roman_persecution_under_Emperor_Constantine_.28AD_313.29">End of Roman persecution under Emperor Constantine (AD 313)</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jesus-Christ-from-Hagia-Sophia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="873" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Jesus-Christ-from-Hagia-Sophia.jpg/230px-Jesus-Christ-from-Hagia-Sophia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Jesus-Christ-from-Hagia-Sophia.jpg/345px-Jesus-Christ-from-Hagia-Sophia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Jesus-Christ-from-Hagia-Sophia.jpg/460px-Jesus-Christ-from-Hagia-Sophia.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jesus-Christ-from-Hagia-Sophia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An example of Byzantine pictorial art, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De%C3%ABsis" title="Deësis">Deësis</a> mosaic at the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia#De.C3.ABsis_mosaic" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>State persecution ceased in the 4th century, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> issued an <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">edict of toleration</a> in 313. On 27 February 380, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> enacted a law establishing <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">Nicene</a> Christianity as the <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">state church of the Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup> From at least the 4th century, Christianity has played a prominent role in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Western_civilization" title="History of Western civilization">shaping of Western civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orlandis_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orlandis-145">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Constantine was also instrumental in the convocation of the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> in 325, which sought to address the <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arian heresy</a> and formulated the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>, which is still used by the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodoxy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a> and many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UMC.E2.80.94Our_Common_Heritage_as_Christians_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-UMC.E2.80.94Our_Common_Heritage_as_Christians-43">[40]</a></sup> Nicaea was the first of a series of <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_council" title="Ecumenical council">Ecumenical (worldwide) Councils</a> which formally defined critical elements of the theology of the Church, notably concerning <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McManners37_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-McManners37-146">[143]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a> did not accept the third and following Ecumenical Councils, and are still separate today.</p>
<p>The presence of Christianity in Africa began in the middle of the 1st century in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, and by the end of the 2nd century in the region around <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mark_the_Evangelist" title="Mark the Evangelist">Mark the Evangelist</a> started the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria</a> in about AD 43.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[146]</a></sup> Important Africans who influenced the early development of Christianity includes <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origen_of_Alexandria" title="Origen of Alexandria">Origen of Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprian" title="Cyprian">Cyprian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athanasius" title="Athanasius">Athanasius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>. The later rise of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> reduced the size and numbers of Christian congregations, leaving only the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Church</a> in Egypt, the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Sudan" title="Christianity in Sudan">Nubian Church</a> in the Sudan (Nobatia, Makuria and Alodia).</p>
<p>In terms of prosperity and cultural life, the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> was one of the peaks in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_history" title="Christian history">Christian history</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_civilization" title="Orthodox civilization">Orthodox civilization</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[147]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> remained the leading city of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_world" title="Christian world">Christian world</a> in size, wealth and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[148]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">There was a renewed interest in classical Greek philosophy</a>, as well as an increase in literary output in vernacular Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-190-218_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-190-218-152">[149]</a></sup> Byzantine art and literature held a pre-eminent place in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, and the cultural impact of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine art</a> on the west during this period was enormous and of long lasting significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browning-1992-218_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browning-1992-218-153">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages">Early Middle Ages</span></h3>
<p>With the decline and fall of the Roman Empire in the west, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papacy" title="Papacy">papacy</a> became a political player, first visible in <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_I" title="Pope Leo I">Pope Leo</a>'s diplomatic dealings with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attila_the_Hun" title="Attila the Hun">Huns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalez-p238_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gonzalez-p238-154">[151]</a></sup> The church also entered into a long period of missionary activity and expansion among the various tribes. While <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arianists</a> instituted the death penalty for practicing pagans (see <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_Verden" title="Massacre of Verden">Massacre of Verden</a> as example), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a> also spread among the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalez-p238_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gonzalez-p238-154">[151]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic peoples</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hungarians" title="Hungarians">Hungarians</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltic_peoples" title="Baltic peoples">Baltic peoples</a>. Christianity has been an important part of the shaping of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">Western civilization</a>, at least since the 4th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_University_Historical_Series_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_University_Historical_Series-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caltron_J.H_Hayas_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Caltron_J.H_Hayas-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orlandis_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Orlandis-145">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Around 500, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Benedict" title="St. Benedict">St. Benedict</a> set out his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monastic_Rule" title="Monastic Rule">Monastic Rule</a>, establishing a system of regulations for the foundation and running of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monasteries" title="Monasteries">monasteries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalez-p238_154-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gonzalez-p238-154">[151]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">Monasticism</a> became a powerful force throughout Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalez-p238_154-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gonzalez-p238-154">[151]</a></sup> and gave rise to many early centers of learning, most famously in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>, contributing to the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a> of the 9th century.</p>
<p>In the 7th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Syria" title="Muslim conquest of Syria">Muslims conquered Syria</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>), <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>. Part of the Muslims' success was due to the exhaustion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_empire" title="Byzantine empire">Byzantine empire</a> in its decades long conflict with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[152]</a></sup> Beginning in the 8th century, with the rise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolingian" title="Carolingian">Carolingian</a> leaders, the papacy began to find greater political support in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankish_Kingdom" title="Frankish Kingdom">Frankish Kingdom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Middle Ages brought about major changes within the church. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_the_Great" title="Pope Gregory the Great">Pope Gregory the Great</a> dramatically reformed <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_polity" title="Ecclesiastical polity">ecclesiastical structure</a> and administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup> In the early 8th century, <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">iconoclasm</a> became a divisive issue, when it was sponsored by the <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantine</a> emperors. The <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Second Ecumenical Council of Nicaea</a> (787) finally pronounced in favor of icons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[155]</a></sup> In the early 10th century, Western Christian monasticism was further rejuvenated through the leadership of the great Benedictine monastery of <a href="/wiki/Cluny_Abbey" title="Cluny Abbey">Cluny</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hebraism, like Hellenism, has been an all-important factor in the development of Western Civilization; Judaism, as the precursor of Christianity, has indirectly had much to do with shaping the ideals and morality of western nations since the Christian era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caltron_J.H_Hayas_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Caltron_J.H_Hayas-12">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_and_Late_Middle_Ages">High and Late Middle Ages</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Passages_d%27outremer_Fr5594,_fol._19r,_Concile_de_Clermont.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="729" data-file-width="752" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Passages_d%27outremer_Fr5594%2C_fol._19r%2C_Concile_de_Clermont.jpg/220px-Passages_d%27outremer_Fr5594%2C_fol._19r%2C_Concile_de_Clermont.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Passages_d%27outremer_Fr5594%2C_fol._19r%2C_Concile_de_Clermont.jpg/330px-Passages_d%27outremer_Fr5594%2C_fol._19r%2C_Concile_de_Clermont.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Passages_d%27outremer_Fr5594%2C_fol._19r%2C_Concile_de_Clermont.jpg/440px-Passages_d%27outremer_Fr5594%2C_fol._19r%2C_Concile_de_Clermont.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Passages_d%27outremer_Fr5594,_fol._19r,_Concile_de_Clermont.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">Pope Urban II</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Clermont" title="Council of Clermont">Council of Clermont</a>, where he preached the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the west, from the 11th century onward, older cathedral schools developed into universities (see <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a>.) The traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_universities" title="Medieval universities">medieval universities</a>—evolved from Catholic and Protestant church schools—then established specialized academic structures for properly educating greater numbers of students as <a href="/wiki/Profession" title="Profession">professionals</a>. Prof. Walter Rüegg, editor of <i><a href="/wiki/A_History_of_the_University_in_Europe" title="A History of the University in Europe">A History of the University in Europe</a></i>, reports that universities then only trained students to become clerics, lawyers, civil servants and physicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Originally teaching only <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universities" title="Universities">universities</a> steadily added subjects including <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a>, becoming the direct ancestors of modern institutions of learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[158]</a></sup> The university is generally regarded as an institution that has its origin in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity" title="History of Christianity">Medieval Christian</a> setting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.C3.BCegg.2C_Walter_1992.2C_pp._XIX_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-R.C3.BCegg.2C_Walter_1992.2C_pp._XIX-162">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verger1999_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-verger1999-163">[160]</a></sup> Prior to the establishment of universities, European higher education took place for hundreds of years in Christian <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_school" title="Cathedral school">cathedral schools</a> or <a href="/wiki/Monastic_school" title="Monastic school">monastic schools</a> (<i>Scholae monasticae</i>), in which <a href="/wiki/Monk" title="Monk">monks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">nuns</a> taught classes; evidence of these immediate forerunners of the later university at many places dates back to the 6th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>Accompanying the rise of the "new towns" throughout Europe, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mendicant_order" title="Mendicant order">mendicant orders</a> were founded, bringing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consecrated_life_(Catholic_Church)" title="Consecrated life (Catholic Church)">consecrated religious life</a> out of the monastery and into the new urban setting. The two principal mendicant movements were the <a href="/wiki/Franciscans" title="Franciscans">Franciscans</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[162]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominicans</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[163]</a></sup> founded by <a href="/wiki/Francis_of_Assisi" title="Francis of Assisi">St. Francis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Dominic" title="St. Dominic">St. Dominic</a> respectively. Both orders made significant contributions to the development of the great universities of Europe. Another new order were the <a href="/wiki/Cistercians" title="Cistercians">Cistercians</a>, whose large isolated monasteries spearheaded the settlement of former wilderness areas. In this period church building and ecclesiastical architecture reached new heights, culminating in the orders of <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic architecture</a> and the building of the great European cathedrals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1095 under the pontificate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_II" title="Urban II">Urban II</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> were launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[165]</a></sup> These were a series of military campaigns in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> and elsewhere, initiated in response to pleas from the Byzantine Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexios_I" title="Alexios I">Alexios I</a> for aid against <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turkish</a> expansion. The Crusades ultimately failed to stifle Islamic aggression and even contributed to Christian enmity with the sacking of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">Fourth Crusade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over a period stretching from the 7th to the 13th century, the Christian Church underwent gradual alienation, resulting in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East-West_Schism" title="East-West Schism">schism</a> dividing it into a so-called Latin or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Christian" title="Western Christian">Western Christian</a> branch, the Roman Catholic Church,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[167]</a></sup> and an <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern</a>, largely Greek, branch, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Church</a>. These two churches disagree on a number of administrative, liturgical and doctrinal issues, most notably <a href="/wiki/Papal_primacy" title="Papal primacy">papal primacy of jurisdiction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orthodox_Information_Centre-Great_Schism_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orthodox_Information_Centre-Great_Schism-171">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SandSp91_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-SandSp91-172">[169]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Lyon" title="Second Council of Lyon">Second Council of Lyon</a> (1274) and the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Florence" title="Council of Florence">Council of Florence</a> (1439) attempted to reunite the churches, but in both cases the Eastern Orthodox refused to implement the decisions and the two principal churches remain in schism to the present day. However, the Roman Catholic Church has achieved union with various <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">smaller eastern churches</a>.</p>
<p>Beginning around 1184, following the crusade against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathars" title="Cathars">Cathar</a> heresy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[170]</a></sup> various institutions, broadly referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Inquisition" title="Inquisition">Inquisition</a>, were established with the aim of suppressing <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> and securing religious and doctrinal unity within Christianity through <a href="/wiki/Religious_conversion" title="Religious conversion">conversion</a> and prosecution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_Reformation_and_Counter-Reformation">Protestant Reformation and Counter-Reformation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:95Thesen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="654" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/95Thesen.jpg/250px-95Thesen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/95Thesen.jpg/375px-95Thesen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/95Thesen.jpg/500px-95Thesen.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:95Thesen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> started the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> in 1517 with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Ninety-Five_Theses" title="The Ninety-Five Theses">Ninety-Five Theses</a>, going against the Catholic interpretation of the Bible.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/European_wars_of_religion" title="European wars of religion">European wars of religion</a></div>
<p>15th-century <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> brought about a renewed interest in ancient and classical learning. Another major schism, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a>, resulted in the splintering of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">Western Christendom</a> into several branches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon-120-121_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simon-120-121-175">[172]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> in 1517 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/95_Theses" title="95 Theses">protested</a> against the sale of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indulgences" title="Indulgences">indulgences</a> and soon moved on to deny several key points of Roman Catholic <a href="/wiki/Doctrine" title="Doctrine">doctrine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simon-176">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other reformers like <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Zwingli</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">Calvin</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">Knox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Arminius" title="Jacobus Arminius">Arminius</a> further criticized Roman Catholic teaching and worship. These challenges developed into the movement called <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>, which repudiated the <a href="/wiki/Papal_primacy" title="Papal primacy">primacy of the pope</a>, the role of tradition, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_sacraments" title="Catholic sacraments">seven sacraments</a> and other doctrines and practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-Simon-176">[173]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">Reformation in England</a> began in 1534, when <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">King Henry VIII</a> had himself <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Act_of_Supremacy" title="Act of Supremacy">declared head</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. Beginning in 1536, the monasteries throughout England, Wales and Ireland were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_monasteries" title="Dissolution of the monasteries">dissolved</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schama_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schama-177">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_M%C3%BCntzer" title="Thomas Müntzer">Thomas Müntzer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Karlstadt" title="Andreas Karlstadt">Andreas Karlstadt</a> and other theologians perceived both the Roman Catholic Church and the confessions of the <a href="/wiki/Magisterial_Reformation" title="Magisterial Reformation">Magisterial Reformation</a> as corrupted. Their activity brought about the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Reformation" title="Radical Reformation">Radical Reformation</a>, which gave birth to various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptist</a> denominations.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1660" data-file-width="1584" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg/230px-Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg/345px-Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg/460px-Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michelangelo%27s_Piet%C3%A0" title="Michelangelo's Pietà">Pietà</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter's Basilica">St. Peter's Basilica</a>, The Catholic Church was among the patronages of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_254_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_254-178">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[177]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Partly in response to the Protestant Reformation, the Roman Catholic Church engaged in a substantial process of reform and renewal, known as the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> or Catholic Reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bokenkotter242_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bokenkotter242-181">[178]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> clarified and reasserted Roman Catholic doctrine. During the following centuries, competition between Roman Catholicism and Protestantism became deeply entangled with political struggles among European states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon3_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simon3-182">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, the discovery of America by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> in 1492 brought about a new wave of missionary activity. Partly from missionary zeal, but under the impetus of <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonial expansion</a> by the European powers, Christianity spread to the Americas, Oceania, <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>.</p>
<p>Throughout Europe, the divides caused by the Reformation led to outbreaks of <a href="/wiki/Religious_violence" title="Religious violence">religious violence</a> and the establishment of separate state churches in Europe. <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> spread into northern, central and eastern parts of present-day Germany, <a href="/wiki/Livonia" title="Livonia">Livonia</a> and Scandinavia. <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a> was established in England in 1534. <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> and its varieties (such as <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterianism</a>) were introduced in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>. <a href="/wiki/Arminianism" title="Arminianism">Arminianism</a> gained followers in the Netherlands and <a href="/wiki/Frisia" title="Frisia">Frisia</a>. Ultimately, these differences led to the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Religious_war" title="Religious war">conflicts</a> in which religion played a key factor. The <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/French_Wars_of_Religion" title="French Wars of Religion">French Wars of Religion</a> are prominent examples. These events intensified the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_debate_on_persecution_and_toleration" title="Christian debate on persecution and toleration">Christian debate on persecution and toleration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[180]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Enlightenment">Post-Enlightenment</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Madonna_and_Child,_Kakure_Kirishitan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="370" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Madonna_and_Child%2C_Kakure_Kirishitan.jpg/230px-Madonna_and_Child%2C_Kakure_Kirishitan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Madonna_and_Child%2C_Kakure_Kirishitan.jpg/345px-Madonna_and_Child%2C_Kakure_Kirishitan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f9/Madonna_and_Child%2C_Kakure_Kirishitan.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Madonna_and_Child,_Kakure_Kirishitan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A depiction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madonna_and_Child" title="Madonna and Child">Madonna and Child</a> in a 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Kakure_Kirishitan" title="Kakure Kirishitan">Kakure Kirishitan</a> <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">Japanese woodcut</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the era known as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Divergence" title="Great Divergence">Great Divergence</a>, when in the West the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" title="Scientific revolution">Scientific revolution</a> brought about great societal changes, Christianity was confronted with various forms of <a href="/wiki/Skepticism" title="Skepticism">skepticism</a> and with certain modern <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">political ideologies</a> such as versions of <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[181]</a></sup> Events ranged from mere <a href="/wiki/Anti-clericalism" title="Anti-clericalism">anti-clericalism</a> to violent outbursts against Christianity such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dechristianisation_of_France_during_the_French_Revolution" title="Dechristianisation of France during the French Revolution">Dechristianisation</a> during the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[182]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> and certain <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> movements, especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_(1917)" title="Russian Revolution (1917)">the Russian Revolution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Persecution of Christians in the Soviet Union">persecution of Christians in the Soviet Union</a> under <a href="/wiki/State_atheism" title="State atheism">state atheism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adappur2000_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adappur2000-188">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>Especially pressing in Europe was the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nation_states" title="Nation states">nation states</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_era" title="Napoleonic era">Napoleonic era</a>. In all European countries, different Christian denominations found themselves in competition, to greater or lesser extents, with each other and with the state. Variables are the relative sizes of the denominations and the religious, political and ideological orientation of the state. Urs Altermatt of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Fribourg" title="University of Fribourg">University of Fribourg</a>, looking specifically at Catholicisms in Europe, identifies four models for the European nations. In traditionally Catholic countries such as <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and to some extent <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, religious and national communities are more or less identical. Cultural symbiosis and separation are found in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, all countries with competing denominations. Competition is found in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and again Switzerland, all countries with minority Catholic populations who to a greater or lesser extent did identify with the nation. Finally, separation between religion (again, specifically Catholicism) and the state is found to a great degree in France and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, countries where the state actively opposed itself to the authority of the Catholic Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>The combined factors of the formation of nation states and <a href="/wiki/Ultramontanism" title="Ultramontanism">ultramontanism</a>, especially in Germany and the Netherlands but also in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> (to a much lesser extent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[188]</a></sup>), often forced Catholic churches, organizations and believers to choose between the national demands of the state and the authority of the Church, specifically the papacy. This conflict came to a head in the <a href="/wiki/First_Vatican_Council" title="First Vatican Council">First Vatican Council</a>, and in Germany would lead directly to the <a href="/wiki/Kulturkampf" title="Kulturkampf">Kulturkampf</a>, where liberals and Protestants under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Bismarck</a> managed to severely restrict Catholic expression and organization.</p>
<p>Christian commitment in Europe dropped as modernity and secularism came into their own in Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[189]</a></sup> particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[190]</a></sup> while religious commitments in America have been generally high in comparison to Europe. The late 20th century has shown the shift of Christian adherence to the Third World and southern hemisphere in general, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">western civilization</a> no longer the chief standard bearer of Christianity.</p>
<p>Some Europeans (including diaspora), <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Indigenous peoples of the Americas</a> and natives of other continents have revived their respective peoples' historical folk religions. Approximately 7.1 to 10% of <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_Christians_and_Arabic-speaking_Christians" title="Arab Christians and Arabic-speaking Christians">Christians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pacini_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pacini-194">[191]</a></sup> most prevalent in Egypt, Syria and Lebanon.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_by_country" title="Christianity by country">Christianity by country</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_population_growth" title="Christian population growth">Christian population growth</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_denominations_by_membership" title="Christian denominations by membership">Christian denominations by membership</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a></div>
<p>With around 2.4 billion adherents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-World-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gordonconwell.edu_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-gordonconwell.edu-6">[5]</a></sup> split into three main branches of Catholic, Protestant and Eastern Orthodox, Christianity is the <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups" title="Major religious groups">world's largest religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PewDec2012_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-PewDec2012-4">[3]</a></sup> The Christian share of the world's population has stood at around 33% for the last hundred years, which says that one in three persons on earth are Christians. This masks a major shift in the demographics of Christianity; large increases in the developing world have been accompanied by substantial declines in the developed world, mainly in Europe and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[192]</a></sup> According to a 2015 <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> study, within the next four decades, Christians will remain the <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups#Largest_religions" title="Major religious groups">world's largest religion</a>; and by 2050, the Christian population is expected to exceed 3 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PewProjections_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-PewProjections-196">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:60</sup></p>
<p>As a percentage of Christians, the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">Orthodoxy</a> (both <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental</a>) are declining, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestants" title="Protestants">Protestants</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> are on the rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pewforum1_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-pewforum1-197">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[196]</a></sup> The so-called <i>popular Protestantism</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[note 4]</a></sup> is one of the fastest growing religious categories in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-201">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barker_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barker-202">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>Christianity is the predominant religion in Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>. In Asia, it is the dominant religion in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">East Timor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[199]</a></sup> However, it is declining in many areas including the <a href="/wiki/Northern_United_States" title="Northern United States">Northern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">Western United States</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[200]</a></sup> Oceania (Australia and New Zealand), northern Europe (including Great Britain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[201]</a></sup> Scandinavia and other places), France, Germany, the Canadian provinces of <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, and parts of Asia (especially the Middle East – due to the <a href="/wiki/Christian_emigration" title="Christian emigration">Christian emigration</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[204]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christianity_in_South_Korea" title="Christianity in South Korea">South Korea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[205]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[206]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[207]</a></sup>).</p>
<p>The Christian population is not decreasing in Brazil, the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern United States</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARIS2008_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARIS2008-212">[208]</a></sup> and the province of <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, Canada,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census2001_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-census2001-213">[209]</a></sup> but the percentage is decreasing. In countries such as Australia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[210]</a></sup> and New Zealand,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2006table28_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-2006table28-215">[211]</a></sup> the Christian population are declining in both numbers and percentage.</p>
<p>Despite the declining numbers, Christianity remains the dominant religion in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_World" title="Western World">Western World</a>, where 70% are Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Christianity_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Christianity-7">[6]</a></sup> A 2011 <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> survey found that 76.2% of <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europeans</a>, 73.3% in <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a> and about 86.0% in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> (90.0% in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> and 77.4% in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>) identified themselves as Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Christianity_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Christianity-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[214]</a></sup> By 2010 about 157 countries and territories in the world had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_majority_countries" title="Christian majority countries">Christian majorities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PewDec2012_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-PewDec2012-4">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, there are many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charismatic_movements" title="Charismatic movements">charismatic movements</a> that have become well established over large parts of the world, especially Africa, Latin America and Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-movedbythespirit_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-movedbythespirit-221">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-222">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CTReview_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-CTReview-223">[219]</a></sup> Since 1900, primarily due to conversion, Protestantism has spread rapidly in Africa, Asia, Oceania and Latin America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_of_Protestantism_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_of_Protestantism-224">[220]</a></sup> From 1960 to 2000, the global growth of the number of reported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Protestants" title="Evangelical Protestants">Evangelical Protestants</a> grew three times the world's population rate, and twice that of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[221]</a></sup> St. Mary's University study estimated about 10.2 million <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convert_to_Christianity" title="Convert to Christianity">convert to Christianity</a> in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-academia.edu_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-academia.edu-226">[222]</a></sup> as well a significant numbers of Muslims converts to Christianity in Afghanistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-state2009_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-state2009-227">[223]</a></sup> Albania,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-academia.edu_226-1"><a href="#cite_note-academia.edu-226">[222]</a></sup> Azerbaijan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[225]</a></sup> Algeria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChristianAction_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChristianAction-231">[227]</a></sup> Belgium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[228]</a></sup> France,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChristianAction_231-1"><a href="#cite_note-ChristianAction-231">[227]</a></sup> Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[229]</a></sup> Iran,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[230]</a></sup> India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChristianAction_231-2"><a href="#cite_note-ChristianAction-231">[227]</a></sup> Indonesia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[231]</a></sup> Malaysia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Musa_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-Musa-236">[232]</a></sup> Morocco,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChristianAction_231-3"><a href="#cite_note-ChristianAction-231">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[233]</a></sup> Russia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChristianAction_231-4"><a href="#cite_note-ChristianAction-231">[227]</a></sup> Netherlands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[234]</a></sup> Saudi Arabia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[235]</a></sup> Tunisia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-academia.edu_226-2"><a href="#cite_note-academia.edu-226">[222]</a></sup> Turkey,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChristianAction_231-5"><a href="#cite_note-ChristianAction-231">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[238]</a></sup> Kazakhstan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[239]</a></sup> Kyrgyzstan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-academia.edu_226-3"><a href="#cite_note-academia.edu-226">[222]</a></sup> Kosovo,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[240]</a></sup> United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[241]</a></sup> and Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[243]</a></sup> It is also reported that Christianity is popular among people of different backgrounds in India (mostly Hindus),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[245]</a></sup> and Malaysia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[246]</a></sup> Mongolia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[247]</a></sup> Nigeria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Religious_Demographic_Profiles_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-Religious_Demographic_Profiles-252">[248]</a></sup> Vietnam,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liberty_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-liberty-253">[249]</a></sup> Singapore,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[250]</a></sup> Indonesia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[252]</a></sup> China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[253]</a></sup> Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christianpost.com_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-christianpost.com-258">[254]</a></sup> and South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>In most countries in the developed world, <a href="/wiki/Church_attendance" title="Church attendance">church attendance</a> among people who continue to identify themselves as Christians has been falling over the last few decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[256]</a></sup> Some sources view this simply as part of a drift away from traditional membership institutions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[257]</a></sup> while others link it to signs of a decline in belief in the importance of religion in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[258]</a></sup> Europe's Christian population, though in decline, still constitutes the largest geographical component of the religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[259]</a></sup> According to data from the 2012 European Social Survey, around a third of <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Europe" title="Christianity in Europe">European Christians</a> say they attend services once a month or more,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-economist-264">[260]</a></sup> Conversely about more than two-thirds of Latin American Christians and according to the <a href="/wiki/World_Values_Survey" title="World Values Survey">World Values Survey</a> about 90% of <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Africa" title="Christianity in Africa">African Christians</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>) said they attended church regularly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_264-1"><a href="#cite_note-economist-264">[260]</a></sup></p>
<p>Christianity, in one form or another, is the sole <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a> of the following nations: Argentina (Roman Catholic),<sup
