class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Argentina_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Argentina-265">[261]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu" title="Tuvalu">Tuvalu</a> (Reformed), <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Tonga</a> (Methodist), Norway (Lutheran),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abcnyheter_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-abcnyheter-266">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[264]</a></sup> Costa Rica (Roman Catholic),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Costa_Rica_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Costa_Rica-269">[265]</a></sup> Kingdom of Denmark (Lutheran),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Denmark_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Denmark-270">[266]</a></sup> England (Anglican),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Centre_for_Citizenship-England_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-Centre_for_Citizenship-England-271">[267]</a></sup> Georgia (Georgian Orthodox),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beliefnet-Georgia_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beliefnet-Georgia-272">[268]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> (Greek Orthodox),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-El_Salvador_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-El_Salvador-273">[269]</a></sup> Iceland (Lutheran),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Iceland_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Iceland-274">[270]</a></sup> Liechtenstein (Roman Catholic),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._Department_of_State-Liechtenstein_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._Department_of_State-Liechtenstein-275">[271]</a></sup> Malta (Roman Catholic),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Malta_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Malta-276">[272]</a></sup> Monaco (Roman Catholic),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Monaco_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Monaco-277">[273]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a> (Roman Catholic).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Vatican_City_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-Vatican_City-278">[274]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are numerous other countries, such as Cyprus, which although do not have an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Established_church" title="Established church">established church</a>, still give official recognition and support to a specific <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">Christian denomination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._Department_of_State-Cyprus_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._Department_of_State-Cyprus-279">[275]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_denominations">Major denominations</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christian_denominations" title="List of Christian denominations">List of Christian denominations</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christian_denominations_by_number_of_members" title="List of Christian denominations by number of members">List of Christian denominations by number of members</a></div>
<p>The three primary divisions of Christianity are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodoxy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhodes_2005_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rhodes_2005-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:14</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-North_Virgina_College.E2.80.94Four_Sects_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-North_Virgina_College.E2.80.94Four_Sects-282">[278]</a></sup> However, there are other Christian groups that do not fit neatly into one of these primary categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Religious_Tolerance.E2.80.94Four_Sects_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-Religious_Tolerance.E2.80.94Four_Sects-283">[279]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a> is accepted as authoritative by most Christian denominations, including the Roman Catholic, Eastern Orthodox, Anglican and major Protestant churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[280]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is a diversity of <a href="/wiki/Doctrine" title="Doctrine">doctrines</a> and practices among groups calling themselves Christian. These groups are sometimes classified under <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">denominations</a>, though for theological reasons many groups reject this classification system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[281]</a></sup> A broader distinction that is sometimes drawn is between <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">Western Christianity</a>, which has its origins in the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a> (Great Schism) of the 11th century.</p>
<p>In addition to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reformed" title="Reformed">Reformed</a> (or Calvinist) branches of the Reformation, there is <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a> after the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptist</a> tradition was largely ostracized by the other Protestant parties at the time, but has achieved a measure of affirmation in more recent history. <a href="/wiki/Adventism" title="Adventism">Adventist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baptist" title="Baptist">Baptist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist">Methodist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentecostal" title="Pentecostal">Pentecostal</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christian_denominations#Protestantism" title="List of Christian denominations">other Protestant</a> confessions arose in the following centuries.</p>
<p>As well as these modern divisions, there were many diverse Christian communities with wildly different Christologies, eschatologies, soteriologies and cosmologies that existed alongside the "Early Church" which is itself a projected concept to indicate which communities were "proto-orthodox", in that their views would become dominate. In many ways, the first three centuries of Christianity was significantly more diverse than the modern Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[282]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_Church">Catholic Church</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franciscus_in_2015.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1599" data-file-width="1209" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Franciscus_in_2015.jpg/220px-Franciscus_in_2015.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Franciscus_in_2015.jpg/330px-Franciscus_in_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Franciscus_in_2015.jpg/440px-Franciscus_in_2015.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franciscus_in_2015.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a>, the current leader of the Catholic Church</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Catholic Church consists of those <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particular_Church" title="Particular Church">particular Churches</a>, headed by bishops, in communion with the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a>, the Bishop of Rome, as its highest authority in matters of faith, morality and Church governance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LumenG_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-LumenG-287">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SandSp1_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-SandSp1-288">[284]</a></sup> Like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodoxy</a>, the Roman Catholic Church, through <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_succession" title="Apostolic succession">apostolic succession</a>, traces its origins to the Christian community founded by Jesus Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatGeographic281_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeographic281-289">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norman11_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norman11-290">[286]</a></sup> Catholics maintain that the "<a href="/wiki/Four_Marks_of_the_Church" title="Four Marks of the Church">one, holy, catholic and apostolic church</a>" founded by Jesus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%22Subsistit_in%22_in_Lumen_Gentium" title='"Subsistit in" in Lumen Gentium'>subsists fully</a> in the Roman Catholic Church, but also acknowledges other Christian churches and communities<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LumenGentium_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-LumenGentium-291">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[288]</a></sup> and works towards <a href="/wiki/Ecumenism" title="Ecumenism">reconciliation</a> among all Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LumenGentium_291-1"><a href="#cite_note-LumenGentium-291">[287]</a></sup> The Catholic faith is detailed in the <i><a href="/wiki/Catechism_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Catechism of the Catholic Church">Catechism of the Catholic Church</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cat_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-cat-293">[289]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[290]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2,834 <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_see" title="Episcopal see">sees</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[291]</a></sup> are grouped into <a href="/wiki/Sui_iuris#Catholic_ecclesiastical_use" title="Sui iuris">24 particular autonomous Churches</a> (the largest of which being the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a>), each with its own distinct traditions regarding the <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a> and the administering the <a href="/wiki/Sacraments_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Sacraments of the Catholic Church">sacraments</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OneFaith71_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-OneFaith71-296">[292]</a></sup> With more than 1.1 billion baptized members, the Catholic Church is the largest Christian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecclesia_(church)" title="Ecclesia (church)">church</a> and represents over half of all Christians as well as one sixth of the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world's population</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-297">[293]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adherents_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adherents-298">[294]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[295]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Orthodox_Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4516" data-file-width="4940" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg/220px-Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg/330px-Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg/440px-Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour" title="Cathedral of Christ the Saviour">Cathedral of Christ the Saviour</a> in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> is the tallest <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Christian</a> church in the world.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Eastern Orthodox Church consists of those churches in communion with the <a href="/wiki/Patriarch" title="Patriarch">Patriarchal</a> Sees of the East, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODCC1199_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODCC1199-300">[296]</a></sup> Like the Roman Catholic Church, the Eastern Orthodox Church also traces its heritage to the foundation of Christianity through <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_succession" title="Apostolic succession">apostolic succession</a> and has an <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_polity" title="Episcopal polity">episcopal</a> structure, though the <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autonomy</a> of its component parts is emphasized, and most of them are national churches. A number of conflicts with Western Christianity over questions of doctrine and authority culminated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East-West_Schism" title="East-West Schism">Great Schism</a>. Eastern Orthodoxy is the second largest single denomination in Christianity, with an estimated 225–300 million adherents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Christianity_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Christianity-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adherents_298-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adherents-298">[294]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aboutWeb_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-aboutWeb-301">[297]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oriental_Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodoxy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodoxy</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodox churches</a> (also called "Old Oriental" churches) are those eastern churches that recognize the first three ecumenical councils—<a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">Nicaea</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Ephesus" title="First Council of Ephesus">Ephesus</a>—but reject the dogmatic definitions of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> and instead espouse a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miaphysite" title="Miaphysite">Miaphysite</a> <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">christology</a>. The Oriental Orthodox communion consists of six groups: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox" title="Syriac Orthodox">Syriac Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox" title="Ethiopian Orthodox">Ethiopian Orthodox</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eritrean_Orthodox" title="Eritrean Orthodox">Eritrean Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malankara_Orthodox_Syrian_Church" title="Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church">Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church</a> (India) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic" title="Armenian Apostolic">Armenian Apostolic</a> churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[298]</a></sup> These six churches, while being in communion with each other are completely independent hierarchically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[299]</a></sup> These churches are generally not in communion with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Churches" title="Eastern Orthodox Churches">Eastern Orthodox Churches</a> with whom they are in dialogue for erecting a communion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sor.cua.edu_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-sor.cua.edu-304">[300]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assyrian_Church_of_the_East">Assyrian Church of the East</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a>, with an unbroken patriarchate established in the 17th century, is an independent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Christian" title="Eastern Christian">Eastern Christian</a> denomination which claims continuity from the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> - in parallell to the Catholic patriarchate established in the 16th century that evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">Chaldean Catholic Church</a>, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic" title="Eastern Catholic">Eastern Catholic</a> church in <a href="/wiki/Full_communion" title="Full communion">full communion</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestantism">Protestantism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a></div>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Protestantism" title="Template:Protestantism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Protestantism" title="Template talk:Protestantism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Protestantism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In the 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, and subsequently <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a>, inaugurated what has come to be called <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>. Luther's primary theological heirs are known as <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutherans</a>. Zwingli and Calvin's heirs are far broader denominationally, and are broadly referred to as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reformed" title="Reformed">Reformed tradition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McManners251_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-McManners251-305">[301]</a></sup> The oldest Protestant groups separated from the Catholic Church in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, often followed by further divisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McManners251_305-1"><a href="#cite_note-McManners251-305">[301]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 18th century, for example, <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodism</a> grew out of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> minister <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a>'s evangelical and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holiness_Movement" title="Holiness Movement">revival movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Methodist_Central_Hall_Westminster.E2.80.94Methodism_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-Methodist_Central_Hall_Westminster.E2.80.94Methodism-306">[302]</a></sup> Several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentecostal" title="Pentecostal">Pentecostal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nondenominational_Christianity" title="Nondenominational Christianity">non-denominational churches</a>, which emphasize the cleansing power of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a>, in turn grew out of Methodism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FYW.E2.80.94Pentecostalism_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-FYW.E2.80.94Pentecostalism-307">[303]</a></sup> Because Methodists, Pentecostals and other evangelicals stress "accepting Jesus as your personal Lord and Savior",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CUMC_Accepting_Christ_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-CUMC_Accepting_Christ-308">[304]</a></sup> which comes from Wesley's emphasis of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Born_again_(Christianity)" title="Born again (Christianity)">New Birth</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UMC_GBGM-The_New_Birth_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-UMC_GBGM-The_New_Birth-309">[305]</a></sup> they often refer to themselves as being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Born_again_Christianity" title="Born again Christianity">born-again</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UMC_GBGM-Grace_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-UMC_GBGM-Grace-310">[306]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warren_Wilson_College.E2.80.94Total_Experience_of_the_Spirit_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warren_Wilson_College.E2.80.94Total_Experience_of_the_Spirit-311">[307]</a></sup></p>
<p>Estimates of the total number of Protestants are very uncertain, but it seems clear that Protestantism is the second largest major group of Christians after Roman Catholicism in number of followers (although the Eastern Orthodox Church is larger than any single Protestant denomination).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adherents_298-2"><a href="#cite_note-Adherents-298">[294]</a></sup> Often that number is put at more than 800 million, corresponding to nearly 40% of world's Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pewforum1_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-pewforum1-197">[194]</a></sup> The majority of Protestants are members of just a handful of denominational families, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Adventism" title="Adventism">Adventists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Reformed (Calvinists)</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[308]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutherans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pentecostalism" title="Pentecostalism">Pentecostals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pewforum1_197-2"><a href="#cite_note-pewforum1-197">[194]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nondenominational_Christianity" title="Nondenominational Christianity">Nondenominational</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">evangelical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charismatic_Movement" title="Charismatic Movement">charismatic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-charismatic_churches" title="Neo-charismatic churches">neo-charismatic</a>, independent and other churches are on the rise, and constitute a significant part of Protestant Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[309]</a></sup></p>
<p>A special grouping are the Anglican churches descended from the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> and organized in the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>. Some Anglican churches consider themselves both Protestant and Catholic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[310]</a></sup> Some Anglicans consider their church a <a href="/wiki/Branch_theory" title="Branch theory">branch of the "One Holy Catholic Church"</a> alongside of the Roman Catholic and Eastern Orthodox churches, a concept rejected by the Roman Catholic Church and some Eastern Orthodox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[311]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[312]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Anglicans, Lutherans and the Reformed branches of Protestantism originated in the <a href="/wiki/Magisterial_Reformation" title="Magisterial Reformation">Magisterial Reformation</a>, other Protestant groups such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptists" title="Anabaptists">Anabaptists</a> originated in the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Reformation" title="Radical Reformation">Radical Reformation</a> and are distinguished by their rejection of infant baptism; they believe in baptism only of adult believers — <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Credobaptism" title="Credobaptism">credobaptism</a>. (Anabaptists are made up mostly of <a href="/wiki/Amish" title="Amish">Amish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mennonites" title="Mennonites">Mennonites</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hutterites" title="Hutterites">Hutterites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Schwarzenau_Brethren" title="Schwarzenau Brethren">Schwarzenau Brethren</a>/<a href="/wiki/German_Baptist" title="German Baptist">German Baptist</a> groups.) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BenedettoDuke2008_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-BenedettoDuke2008-317">[313]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some groups of individuals who hold basic Protestant tenets identify themselves simply as "Christians" or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Born-again" title="Born-again">born-again</a> Christians". They typically distance themselves from the <a href="/wiki/Confessionalism_(religion)" title="Confessionalism (religion)">confessionalism</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creedalism</a> of other Christian communities<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[314]</a></sup> by calling themselves "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-denominational_Christianity" title="Non-denominational Christianity">non-denominational</a>" or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical" title="Evangelical">evangelical</a>". Often founded by individual pastors, they have little affiliation with historic denominations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pew_Forum_on_Religion_.26_Public_Life_.2F_U.S._Religious_Landscape_Survey_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Forum_on_Religion_.26_Public_Life_.2F_U.S._Religious_Landscape_Survey-319">[315]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Protestant_branches.svg" title="Historical chart of the main Protestant branches"><img alt="Historical chart of the main Protestant branches" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="800" height="416" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Protestant_branches.svg/650px-Protestant_branches.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Protestant_branches.svg/975px-Protestant_branches.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Protestant_branches.svg/1300px-Protestant_branches.svg.png 2x" width="650"/></a></div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restorationism">Restorationism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Restorationism" title="Restorationism">Restorationism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Priesthood03080u.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3010" data-file-width="2000" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Priesthood03080u.jpg/180px-Priesthood03080u.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Priesthood03080u.jpg/270px-Priesthood03080u.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Priesthood03080u.jpg/360px-Priesthood03080u.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Priesthood03080u.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 19th-century drawing of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cowdery" title="Oliver Cowdery">Oliver Cowdery</a> receiving the <a href="/wiki/Aaronic_priesthood_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Aaronic priesthood (Latter Day Saints)">Aaronic priesthood</a> from <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>. <a href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saint_movement" title="Latter Day Saint movement">Latter Day Saints</a> believe that the <a href="/wiki/Priest#Christianity" title="Priest">Priesthood</a> ceased to exist after the death of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" title="Apostle (Christian)">Apostles</a> and therefore needed to be <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Restoration (Latter Day Saints)">restored</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Second_Great_Awakening" title="Second Great Awakening">Second Great Awakening</a>, a period of religious revival that occurred in the United States during the early 1800s, saw the development of a number of unrelated churches. They generally saw themselves as <a href="/wiki/Restorationism" title="Restorationism">restoring</a> the original church of Jesus Christ rather than reforming one of the existing churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[316]</a></sup> A common belief held by Restorationists was that the other divisions of Christianity had introduced doctrinal defects into Christianity, which was known as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Apostasy" title="Great Apostasy">Great Apostasy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Religious_Tolerance.E2.80.94Restorationism_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-Religious_Tolerance.E2.80.94Restorationism-321">[317]</a></sup> In Asia, <a href="/wiki/Iglesia_ni_Cristo" title="Iglesia ni Cristo">Iglesia ni Cristo</a> is a known restorationist religion that was established during the early 1900s.</p>
<p>Some of the churches originating during this period are historically connected to early 19th-century camp meetings in the Midwest and Upstate New York. American <a href="/wiki/Millennialism" title="Millennialism">Millennialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adventism" title="Adventism">Adventism</a>, which arose from Evangelical Protestantism, influenced the <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a> movement and, as a reaction specifically to <a href="/wiki/William_Miller_(preacher)" title="William Miller (preacher)">William Miller</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist_Church" title="Seventh-day Adventist Church">Seventh-day Adventists</a>. Others, including the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church_(Disciples_of_Christ)" title="Christian Church (Disciples of Christ)">Christian Church (Disciples of Christ)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Christian_Church_in_Canada" title="Evangelical Christian Church in Canada">Evangelical Christian Church in Canada</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[318]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[319]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Churches_of_Christ" title="Churches of Christ">Churches of Christ</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Christian_churches_and_churches_of_Christ" title="Christian churches and churches of Christ">Christian churches and churches of Christ</a>, have their roots in the contemporaneous Stone-Campbell <a href="/wiki/Restoration_Movement" title="Restoration Movement">Restoration Movement</a>, which was centered in Kentucky and Tennessee. Other groups originating in this time period include the <a href="/wiki/Christadelphians" title="Christadelphians">Christadelphians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saint_movement" title="Latter Day Saint movement">Latter Day Saint movement</a>. While the churches originating in the Second Great Awakening have some superficial similarities, their doctrine and practices vary significantly.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<p>Various smaller <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_Catholic" title="Independent Catholic">Independent Catholic</a> communities, such as the <a href="/wiki/Old_Catholic_Church" title="Old Catholic Church">Old Catholic Church</a>, include the word <i><a href="/wiki/Catholic_(term)" title="Catholic (term)">Catholic</a></i> in their title, and arguably have more or less liturgical practices in common with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, but are no longer in <a href="/wiki/Full_communion" title="Full communion">full communion</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Esoteric_Christianity" title="Esoteric Christianity">Esoteric Christians</a> regard Christianity as a <a href="/wiki/Western_esotericism" title="Western esotericism">mystery religion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[320]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Besant_2001_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-Besant_2001-325">[321]</a></sup> and profess the existence and possession of certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Esotericism" title="Esotericism">esoteric</a> doctrines or practices,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[322]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[323]</a></sup> hidden from the public but accessible only to a narrow circle of "enlightened", "initiated", or highly educated people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[324]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[325]</a></sup> Some of the esoteric Christian institutions include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rosicrucian_Fellowship" title="Rosicrucian Fellowship">Rosicrucian Fellowship</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Anthroposophical_Society" title="Anthroposophical Society">Anthroposophical Society</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martinism" title="Martinism">Martinism</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Messianic_Judaism" title="Messianic Judaism">Messianic Judaism</a> (or Messianic Movement) is the name of a Christian movement comprising a number of streams, whose members may consider themselves Jewish. The movement originated in the 1960s and 1970s, and it blends elements of religious Jewish practice with evangelical Christianity. Messianic Judaism affirms Christian creeds such as the messiahship and divinity of "Yeshua" (the Hebrew name of Jesus) and the Triune Nature of God, while also adhering to some Jewish dietary laws and customs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ariel2006p208_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ariel2006p208-330">[326]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_culture">Christian culture</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Christian_culture" title="Christian culture">Christian culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Role_of_Christianity_in_civilization" title="Role of Christianity in civilization">Role of Christianity in civilization</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protestant_culture" title="Protestant culture">Protestant culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Christian" title="Cultural Christian">Cultural Christian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christian_influences_in_Islam" title="Christian influences in Islam">Christian influences in Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Collage-Christian-culture.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="564" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Collage-Christian-culture.jpg/275px-Collage-Christian-culture.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Collage-Christian-culture.jpg/413px-Collage-Christian-culture.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Collage-Christian-culture.jpg/550px-Collage-Christian-culture.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Collage-Christian-culture.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Set of pictures showcasing Christian culture and famous Christian leaders</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>, throughout most of its history, has been nearly equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Christian_culture" title="Christian culture">Christian culture</a>, and a large portion of the population of the Western hemisphere can be described as cultural Christians. The notion of "<a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>" and the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_World" title="Western World">Western World</a>" has been intimately connected with the concept of "<a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christianity and Christendom</a>" many even attribute Christianity for being the link that created a unified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_identity" title="European identity">European identity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[327]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though Western culture contained several polytheistic religions during its early years under the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman empires</a>, as the centralized Roman power waned, the dominance of the Catholic Church was the only consistent force in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1994_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1994-332">[328]</a></sup> Until the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[329]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_culture" title="Christian culture">Christian culture</a> guided the course of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1994_332-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1994-332">[328]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[330]</a></sup> Christian disciplines of the respective arts have subsequently developed into <a href="/wiki/Christian_philosophy" title="Christian philosophy">Christian philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_art" title="Christian art">Christian art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_music" title="Christian music">Christian music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_literature" title="Christian literature">Christian literature</a> etc.</p>
<p>Christianity has had a significant impact on <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a> as the church created the bases of the Western system of education,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[331]</a></sup> and was the sponsor of founding <a href="/wiki/Medieval_university" title="Medieval university">universities</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>; as the <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">university</a> is generally regarded as an institution that has its origin in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity" title="History of Christianity">Medieval Christian</a> setting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.C3.BCegg.2C_Walter_1992.2C_pp._XIX_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-R.C3.BCegg.2C_Walter_1992.2C_pp._XIX-162">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verger1999_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-verger1999-163">[160]</a></sup> Historically, Christianity has often been a patron of <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>. It has been prolific in the foundation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schools" title="Schools">schools</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universities" title="Universities">universities</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hospitals" title="Hospitals">hospitals</a>, and many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_Catholic_cleric-scientists" title="List of Roman Catholic cleric-scientists">Catholic clergy</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hough_p68_336-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hough_p68-336">[332]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Jesuit_scientists" title="List of Jesuit scientists">Jesuits</a> in particular,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoods2005109_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoods2005109-337">[333]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[334]</a></sup> have been active in the sciences throughout history and have made significant contributions to the <a href="/wiki/Christian_attitudes_towards_science" title="Christian attitudes towards science">development of science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[335]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> also has had an important influence on science. According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merton_Thesis" title="Merton Thesis">Merton Thesis</a>, there was a positive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Correlation" title="Correlation">correlation</a> between the rise of English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puritanism" title="Puritanism">Puritanism</a> and German <a href="/wiki/Pietism" title="Pietism">Pietism</a> on the one hand and early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Experimental_science" title="Experimental science">experimental science</a> on the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sztompka2003_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-sztompka2003-340">[336]</a></sup> The Civilizing influence of Christianity includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_welfare" title="Social welfare">social welfare</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[337]</a></sup> founding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hospitals" title="Hospitals">hospitals</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[338]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a> (as the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_work_ethic" title="Protestant work ethic">Protestant work ethic</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[339]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[340]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[341]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BF_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-BF-346">[342]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[343]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ablution_in_Christianity" title="Ablution in Christianity">personal hygiene</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[344]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[345]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Family" title="Family">family</a> life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[346]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Christians" title="Eastern Christians">Eastern Christians</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>) contributed to the Arab <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Civilization</a> during the reign of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ummayads" title="Ummayads">Ummayad</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasids" title="Abbasids">Abbasid</a> by translating works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophers" title="Greek philosophers">Greek philosophers</a> to <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> and afterwards to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[347]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[348]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[349]</a></sup> They also excelled in <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[350]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[351]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-356"><a href="#cite_note-356">[352]</a></sup> And many scholars of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a> were of Christian background.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[353]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lists_of_Christians" title="Lists of Christians">Christians</a> have made a myriad of contributions to <a href="/wiki/Progress_(history)" title="Progress (history)">human progress</a> in a broad and diverse range of fields,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[354]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Christian_philosophy" title="Christian philosophy">philosophy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[355]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christians_in_science_and_technology" title="List of Christians in science and technology">science and technology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hough_p68_336-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hough_p68-336">[332]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[356]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[357]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[358]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-363"><a href="#cite_note-363">[359]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_Catholic_Church_artists" title="List of Roman Catholic Church artists">fine arts and architecture</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[360]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Catholic_authors" title="List of Catholic authors">literatures</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Music" title="Christian Music">music</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall100_365-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hall100-365">[361]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">business</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[362]</a></sup> According to <i>100 Years of Nobel Prizes</i> a review of Nobel prizes award between 1901 and 2000 reveals that (65.4%) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prizes" title="Nobel Prizes">Nobel Prizes</a> Laureates, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Christian_Nobel_laureates" title="List of Christian Nobel laureates">have identified Christianity</a> in its various forms as their religious preference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nobel_Prize_367-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel_Prize-367">[363]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Postchristianity" title="Postchristianity">Postchristianity</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[364]</a></sup> is the term for the decline of Christianity, particularly <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Europe" title="Christianity in Europe">in Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Canada" title="Religion in Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Australia" title="Christianity in Australia">Australia</a> and to a minor degree the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Cone" title="Southern Cone">Southern Cone</a>, in the 20th and 21st centuries, considered in terms of <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">postmodernism</a>. It refers to the loss of Christianity's monopoly on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Values" title="Values">values</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_view" title="World view">world view</a> in historically Christian societies.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cultural_Christian" title="Cultural Christian">Cultural Christians</a> are secular people with a Christian heritage who may not believe in the religious claims of Christianity, but who retain an affinity for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_pop_culture" title="Christian pop culture">popular culture</a>, art, <a href="/wiki/Christian_music" title="Christian music">music</a> and so on related to it. Another frequent application of the term is to distinguish political groups in areas of mixed religious backgrounds.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecumenism">Ecumenism</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ecumenism" title="Ecumenism">Ecumenism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taiz%C3%A9_prayer.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Taiz%C3%A9_prayer.JPG/220px-Taiz%C3%A9_prayer.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Taiz%C3%A9_prayer.JPG/330px-Taiz%C3%A9_prayer.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Taiz%C3%A9_prayer.JPG/440px-Taiz%C3%A9_prayer.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taiz%C3%A9_prayer.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ecumenical worship service at the <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastery</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taiz%C3%A9" title="Taizé">Taizé</a> in France</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Christian groups and denominations have long expressed ideals of being reconciled, and in the 20th century, Christian <a href="/wiki/Ecumenism" title="Ecumenism">ecumenism</a> advanced in two ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McManners581_369-0"><a href="#cite_note-McManners581-369">[365]</a></sup> One way was greater cooperation between groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/World_Evangelical_Alliance" title="World Evangelical Alliance">World Evangelical Alliance</a> founded in 1846 in London or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Missionary_Conference" title="Edinburgh Missionary Conference">Edinburgh Missionary Conference</a> of Protestants in 1910, the Justice, Peace and Creation Commission of the <a href="/wiki/World_Council_of_Churches" title="World Council of Churches">World Council of Churches</a> founded in 1948 by Protestant and Orthodox churches, and similar national councils like the <a href="/wiki/National_Council_of_Churches_in_Australia" title="National Council of Churches in Australia">National Council of Churches in Australia</a> which includes Roman Catholics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McManners581_369-1"><a href="#cite_note-McManners581-369">[365]</a></sup></p>
<p>The other way was institutional union with <a href="/wiki/United_and_uniting_churches" title="United and uniting churches">United and uniting churches</a>, a practice that can be traced back to unions between Lutherans and Calvinists in early 19th-century Germany. Congregationalist, Methodist and Presbyterian churches united in 1925 to form the <a href="/wiki/United_Church_of_Canada" title="United Church of Canada">United Church of Canada</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[366]</a></sup> and in 1977 to form the <a href="/wiki/Uniting_Church_in_Australia" title="Uniting Church in Australia">Uniting Church in Australia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_South_India" title="Church of South India">Church of South India</a> was formed in 1947 by the union of Anglican, Baptist, Methodist, Congregationalist and Presbyterian churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[367]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ecumenical, <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monastic</a> <a href="/wiki/Taiz%C3%A9_Community" title="Taizé Community">Taizé Community</a> is notable for being composed of more than one hundred <a href="/wiki/Monk" title="Monk">brothers</a> from Protestant and Catholic traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taize_372-0"><a href="#cite_note-taize-372">[368]</a></sup> The community emphasizes the reconciliation of all denominations and its main church, located in <a href="/wiki/Taiz%C3%A9,_Sa%C3%B4ne-et-Loire" title="Taizé, Saône-et-Loire">Taizé, Saône-et-Loire</a>, France, is named the "Church of Reconciliation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taize_372-1"><a href="#cite_note-taize-372">[368]</a></sup> The community is internationally known, attracting over 100,000 young pilgrims annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-373"><a href="#cite_note-373">[369]</a></sup></p>
<p>Steps towards reconciliation on a global level were taken in 1965 by the Roman Catholic and Orthodox churches mutually revoking the excommunications that marked their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East-West_Schism" title="East-West Schism">Great Schism</a> in 1054;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-374"><a href="#cite_note-374">[370]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican_Roman_Catholic_International_Commission" title="Anglican Roman Catholic International Commission">Anglican Roman Catholic International Commission</a> (ARCIC) working towards full communion between those churches since 1970;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[371]</a></sup> and some <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_World_Federation" title="Lutheran World Federation">Lutheran</a> and Roman Catholic churches signing the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Declaration_on_the_Doctrine_of_Justification" title="Joint Declaration on the Doctrine of Justification">Joint Declaration on the Doctrine of Justification</a> in 1999 to address conflicts at the root of the Protestant Reformation. In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/World_Methodist_Council" title="World Methodist Council">World Methodist Council</a>, representing all Methodist denominations, adopted the declaration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-376"><a href="#cite_note-376">[372]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Apologetics_and_criticism">Apologetics and criticism</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Christian_apologetics" title="Christian apologetics">Christian apologetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Christianity" title="Criticism of Christianity">Criticism of Christianity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SummaTheologiae.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="625" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/SummaTheologiae.jpg/200px-SummaTheologiae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/SummaTheologiae.jpg/300px-SummaTheologiae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/SummaTheologiae.jpg/400px-SummaTheologiae.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SummaTheologiae.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A copy of the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa Theologica</a></i>, a famous Christian apologetic work</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Criticism of Christianity and Christians goes back to the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Age" title="Apostolic Age">Apostolic Age</a>, with the New Testament recording friction between the followers of Jesus and the <a href="/wiki/Pharisees" title="Pharisees">Pharisees</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scribes" title="Scribes">scribes</a> (e.g. <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblica.com/en-us/bible/online-bible/?osis=niv:Matthew.15:1–20:1" rel="nofollow">Matthew 15:1-20</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblica.com/en-us/bible/online-bible/?osis=niv:Mark.7:1–23:1" rel="nofollow">Mark 7:1–23</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[373]</a></sup> In the 2nd century, Christianity was criticized by the Jews on various grounds, e.g. that the prophecies of the Hebrew Bible could not have been fulfilled by Jesus, given that he did not have a successful life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-378"><a href="#cite_note-378">[374]</a></sup> Additionally a sacrifice to remove sins in advance, for everyone or as a human being, did not fit to the Jewish sacrifice ritual, furthermore <a href="/wiki/God_in_Judaism" title="God in Judaism">God</a> is said to judge people on their deeds instead of their beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-379"><a href="#cite_note-379">[375]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[376]</a></sup> One of the first comprehensive attacks on Christianity came from the Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Celsus" title="Celsus">Celsus</a>, who wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_True_Word" title="The True Word">The True Word</a></i>, a polemic criticizing Christians as being unprofitable members of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-381"><a href="#cite_note-381">[377]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the 3rd century, criticism of Christianity had mounted, partly as a defense against it. Wild rumors about Christians were widely circulated, claiming that they were <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheists</a> and that, as part of their rituals, they devoured human infants and engaged in incestuous orgies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-382"><a href="#cite_note-382">[378]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-383"><a href="#cite_note-383">[379]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonist</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(philosopher)" title="Porphyry (philosopher)">Porphyry</a> wrote the fifteen-volume <i>Adversus Christianos</i> as a comprehensive attack on Christianity, in part building on the pre-Christian concepts of <a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-384"><a href="#cite_note-384">[380]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-385"><a href="#cite_note-385">[381]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the 12th century, the <a href="/wiki/Mishneh_Torah" title="Mishneh Torah">Mishneh Torah</a> (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">Rabbi</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moses_Maimonides" title="Moses Maimonides">Moses Maimonides</a>) was criticizing Christianity on the grounds of idol worship, in that Christians attributed divinity to Jesus who had a physical body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-386"><a href="#cite_note-386">[382]</a></sup> In the 19th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nietzsche" title="Nietzsche">Nietzsche</a> began to write a series of polemics on the "unnatural" teachings of Christianity (e.g. sexual abstinence), and continued his criticism of Christianity to the end of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-387"><a href="#cite_note-387">[383]</a></sup> In the 20th century, the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a> expressed his criticism of Christianity in <i><a href="/wiki/Why_I_Am_Not_a_Christian" title="Why I Am Not a Christian">Why I Am Not a Christian</a></i>, formulating his rejection of Christianity in the setting of logical arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[384]</a></sup></p>
<p>Criticism of Christianity continues to date, e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> theologians criticize the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> held by most Christians, stating that this doctrine in effect assumes that there are three Gods, running against the basic tenet of <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-389"><a href="#cite_note-389">[385]</a></sup> New Testament scholar <a href="/wiki/Robert_M._Price" title="Robert M. Price">Robert M. Price</a> has outlined the possibility that some Bible stories are based partly on myth in "The Christ Myth Theory and its problems".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-390"><a href="#cite_note-390">[386]</a></sup></p>
<p>Christian apologetics aims to present a <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">rational</a> basis for Christianity. The word "apologetic" comes from the Greek word "apologeomai", meaning "in defense of". Christian apologetics has taken many forms over the centuries, starting with Paul the Apostle. The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> presented five arguments for God's existence in the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa Theologica</a></i>, while his <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_Gentiles" title="Summa contra Gentiles">Summa contra Gentiles</a></i> was a major apologetic work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[387]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-392"><a href="#cite_note-392">[388]</a></sup> Another famous apologist, <a href="/wiki/G._K._Chesterton" title="G. K. Chesterton">G. K. Chesterton</a>, wrote in the early twentieth century about the benefits of religion and, specifically, Christianity. Famous for his use of paradox, Chesterton explained that while Christianity had the most mysteries, it was the most practical religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[389]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-394"><a href="#cite_note-394">[390]</a></sup> He pointed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Role_of_the_Christian_Church_in_civilization" title="Role of the Christian Church in civilization">advance of Christian civilizations</a> as proof of its practicality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chesterton.org_395-0"><a href="#cite_note-chesterton.org-395">[391]</a></sup> The physicist and priest <a href="/wiki/John_Polkinghorne" title="John Polkinghorne">John Polkinghorne</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Questions_of_Truth" title="Questions of Truth">Questions of Truth</a></i> discusses the subject of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religion_and_science" title="Religion and science">religion and science</a>, a topic that other Christian apologists such as <a href="/wiki/Ravi_Zacharias" title="Ravi Zacharias">Ravi Zacharias</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Lennox" title="John Lennox">John Lennox</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Lane_Craig" title="William Lane Craig">William Lane Craig</a> have engaged, with the latter two men opining that the <a href="/wiki/Religious_interpretations_of_the_Big_Bang_theory" title="Religious interpretations of the Big Bang theory">inflationary Big Bang model</a> is evidence for the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howson2011_396-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howson2011-396">[392]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">From <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">Χριστός</span> <i><a href="/wiki/Christ_(title)" title="Christ (title)">Khristós</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latinization_of_Greek" title="Latinization of Greek">Latinized</a> as <i>Christus</i>), translating <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> מָשִׁיחַ, <i><a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Māšîăḥ</a></i>, meaning "the <a href="/wiki/Anointing" title="Anointing">anointed</a> one", with the <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">Latin</a> suffixes <i>-ian</i> and <i>-itas</i>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-name-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-name_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-name_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The term "Christian" (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Χριστιανός</span>) was first used in reference to Jesus's <a href="/wiki/Disciple_(Christianity)" title="Disciple (Christianity)">disciples</a> in the city of <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a><sup class="nourlexpansion reference"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts+11%3A26&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">[Acts 11:26]</a></sup> about 44 AD, meaning "followers of Christ". The name was given by the non-Jewish inhabitants of Antioch to the disciples of Jesus. In the New Testament the names by which the disciples were known among themselves were "brethren", "the faithful", "elect", "saints", and "believers". The earliest recorded use of the term "Christianity" (Greek <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Χριστιανισμός</span>) was by <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_of_Antioch" title="Ignatius of Antioch">Ignatius of Antioch</a>, around 100 AD.<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_Elwell.2FComfort._.27.27Tyndale_Bible_Dictionary.27.27.2C_pp._266.2C_828."><a class="external autonumber" href="//en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christianity#endnote_Elwell.2FComfort._Tyndale_Bible_Dictionary.2C_pp._266.2C_828.">[1]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Good news" is a translation of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> term <span lang="grc-gre" xml:lang="grc-gre">εὐαγγέλιον</span> <i>euangélion</i>, from which the terms <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical" title="Evangelical">evangelical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evangelism" title="Evangelism">evangelism</a> derive.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A flexible term; defined as all forms of Protestantism with the notable exception of the historical denominations deriving directly from the Protestant Reformation.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-Cambridge_University_Historical_Series-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Cambridge_University_Historical_Series_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Cambridge_University_Historical_Series_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Cambridge University Historical Series, <i>An Essay on Western Civilization in Its Economic Aspects</i>, p.40: Hebraism, like Hellenism, has been an all-important factor in the development of Western Civilization; Judaism, as the precursor of Christianity, has indirectly had had much to do with shaping the ideals and morality of western nations since the christian era.</span></li>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">18 October</span> 2007</span>. <q>The enthusiasm for evangelization among the Christians was also accompanied by the awareness that the most immediate problem to solve was how to serve the huge number of new <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Christianity" title="Conversion to Christianity">converts</a>. Simatupang said, if the number of the Christians were double or triple, then the number of the ministers should also be doubled or tripled and the tole of the laity should be maximized and Christian service to society through schools, universities, hospitals and orphanages, should be increased. In addition, for him the Christian mission should be involved in the struggle for justice amid the process of modernization.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Muslim-Christian+Relations&amp;rft.pub=Amsterdam+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=978-90-5356-938-2&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dg2AtOlJMPTUC%26pg%3DPA52&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChristianity"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">18 October</span> 2007</span>. <q>Theologians, bishops, and preachers urged the Christian community to be as compassionate as their God was, reiterating that creation was for all of humanity. They also accepted and developed the identification of Christ with the poor and the requisite Christian duty to the poor. Religious congregations and individual charismatic leaders promoted the development of a number of helping institutions-hospitals, hospices for pilgrims, orphanages, shelters for unwed mothers-that laid the foundation for the modern "large network of hospitals, orphanages and schools, to serve the poor and society at large."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Doing+Faith+Justice&amp;rft.pub=Paulist+Press&amp;rft.date=2004-05-01&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8091-4227-9&amp;rft.au=Fred+Kammer&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3DWmuV6g0yR3sC%26pg%3DPA77%26dq%3Dchristianity%2Bserve%2Bothers%2Bhospitals%2Borphanages%2Bschools%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChristianity"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Examples of ante-Nicene statements</i>:</span>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><span class="reference-text">Hence all the power of magic became dissolved; and every bond of wickedness was destroyed, men's ignorance was taken away, and the old kingdom abolished God Himself appearing in the form of a man, for the renewal of eternal life.</span></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><span class="reference-text">— <cite>St. Ignatius of Antioch in <i>Letter to the Ephesians</i>, ch.4, shorter version, Roberts-Donaldson translation</cite></span></div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><span class="reference-text">We have also as a Physician the Lord our God Jesus the Christ the only-begotten Son and Word, before time began, but who afterwards became also man, of Mary the virgin. For 'the Word was made flesh.' Being incorporeal, He was in the body; being impassible, He was in a passable body; being immortal, He was in a mortal body; being life, He became subject to corruption, that He might free our souls from death and corruption, and heal them, and might restore them to health, when they were diseased with ungodliness and wicked lusts</span></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><span class="reference-text">— <cite>St. Ignatius of Antioch in <i>Letter to the Ephesians</i>, ch.7, shorter version, Roberts-Donaldson translation</cite></span></div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><span class="reference-text">The Church, though dispersed throughout the whole world, even to the ends of the earth, has received from the apostles and their disciples this faith: ...one God, the Father Almighty, Maker of heaven, and earth, and the sea, and all things that are in them; and in one Christ Jesus, the Son of God, who became incarnate for our salvation; and in the Holy Spirit, who proclaimed through the prophets the dispensations of God, and the advents, and the birth from a virgin, and the passion, and the resurrection from the dead, and the ascension into heaven in the flesh of the beloved Christ Jesus, our Lord, and His manifestation from heaven in the glory of the Father 'to gather all things in one,' and to raise up anew all flesh of the whole human race, in order that to Christ Jesus, our Lord, and God, and Savior, and King, according to the will of the invisible Father, 'every knee should bow, of things in heaven, and things in earth, and things under the earth, and that every tongue should confess; to him, and that He should execute just judgment towards all...</span></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><span class="reference-text">— <cite>St. Irenaeus in <i>Against Heresies</i>, ch.X, v.I, <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFDonaldson1950">Donaldson, Sir James (1950), <i>Ante Nicene Fathers, Volume 1: Apostolic Fathers, Justin Martyr, Irenaeus</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_B._Eerdmans_Publishing_Co." title="William B. Eerdmans Publishing Co.">William B. Eerdmans Publishing Co.</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0802880871" title="Special:BookSources/978-0802880871">978-0802880871</a></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Ante+Nicene+Fathers%2C+Volume+1%3A+Apostolic+Fathers%2C+Justin+Martyr%2C+Irenaeus&amp;rft.pub=William+B.+Eerdmans+Publishing+Co.&amp;rft.date=1950&amp;rft.isbn=978-0802880871&amp;rft.aulast=Donaldson&amp;rft.aufirst=Sir+James&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChristianity"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></cite></span></div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>For, in the name of God, the Father and Lord of the universe, and of our Savior Jesus Christ, and of the Holy Spirit, they then receive the washing with water</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Justin Martyr in <i>First Apology</i>, ch. LXI, <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFDonaldson1950">Donaldson, Sir James (1950), <i>Ante Nicene Fathers, Volume 1: Apostolic Fathers, Justin Martyr, Irenaeus</i>, Wm. B. Eerdmans Publishing Company, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0802880871" title="Special:BookSources/978-0802880871">978-0802880871</a></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Ante+Nicene+Fathers%2C+Volume+1%3A+Apostolic+Fathers%2C+Justin+Martyr%2C+Irenaeus&amp;rft.pub=Wm.+B.+Eerdmans+Publishing+Company&amp;rft.date=1950&amp;rft.isbn=978-0802880871&amp;rft.aulast=Donaldson&amp;rft.aufirst=Sir+James&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChristianity"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></cite></div>
</blockquote>
